\chapter{QCD Renormalization, Asymptotic Freedom, and Deep Inelastic Scattering}
\label{ch:volume-ii-19}
\ConventionsLorentzian


\section{QCD as a Renormalized Gauge Theory}

Quantum chromodynamics in four dimensions is Yang--Mills theory with gauge
group \(SU(N_c)\) coupled to \(N_f\) Dirac fermions in the fundamental
representation.  Write the quark fields as
\[
  q^i_{I\alpha}(x),
  \qquad
  i=1,\ldots,N_c,\quad I=1,\ldots,N_f,
\]
where \(i\) is color, \(I\) is flavor, and \(\alpha\) is a spinor index.  We
use the Hermitian-generator normalization fixed in
Chapter~\ref{ch:vol2-classical-yang-mills-matter}, specifically
\eqref{eq:ym-lie-algebra-conventions}, \eqref{eq:ym-trace-kappa}, and
\eqref{eq:ym-lagrangian-density},
\[
  \operatorname{tr}_{\square}(t^a t^b)=\delta^{ab}.
\]
The group-theoretic constants used in renormalization are defined by
\begin{equation}
  f^a{}_{cd}f^b{}_{cd}=C_A\delta^{ab},
  \qquad
  \operatorname{tr}_{R}(t_R^a t_R^b)=T_R\delta^{ab},
  \qquad
  t_R^a t_R^a=C_R\mathbf 1_R .
  \label{eq:qcd-casimir-definitions}
\end{equation}
For \(SU(N_c)\),
\[
  C_A=2N_c,\qquad
  T_F=1,\qquad
  C_F=\frac{N_c^2-1}{N_c}.
\]
These are twice the values often quoted with generators \(T^a\) normalized by
\(\operatorname{tr}_{\square}(T^aT^b)=\frac12\delta^{ab}\).  The physical
matrix-valued connection is unchanged; only the component coordinates and the
coupling coordinate are rescaled.  In the present convention the gauge action is
\(-\frac1{4g^2}\operatorname{tr}(F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu})\).

The renormalized coupling \(g(\mu)\) depends on a scale \(\mu\) and on the
renormalization scheme.  The beta function is
\[
  \beta(g):=\mu\frac{\dd g}{\dd\mu}.
\]
The first coefficient is obtained below from the background-field 1PI
effective action.  For \(N_f\) Dirac fermions in a representation \(R\), in a
mass-independent scheme the result is
\begin{equation}
  \beta(g)
  =
  -\frac{g^3}{16\pi^2}
  \left(
  \frac{11}{3}C_A-\frac{4}{3}T_R N_f
  \right)
  +O(g^5).
  \label{eq:qcd-one-loop-beta-general}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
  \beta(g)
  =
  -\frac{g^3}{24\pi^2}
  \left(
  11N_c-2N_f
  \right)
  +O(g^5)
  \label{eq:qcd-one-loop-beta-fundamental}
\end{equation}
for fundamental quarks in this trace normalization.

\section{Ordinary Covariant-Gauge Green-Function Rules}

The covariant gauge-fixed rules used in ordinary Yang--Mills perturbation
theory are rules for time-ordered Green functions of the local variables
\(A_\mu^a,c^a,\bar c^a,q,\bar q\).  Their physical interpretation is supplied
by the BRST cohomology and by gauge-invariant operators, but the local rules
themselves are needed for renormalization and for comparing different gauges.

In this section write \(g\) for \(g_{\mathrm{YM}}\), and use the normalization
\eqref{eq:ym-lagrangian-density},
\[
  \mathcal L_{\mathrm{YM}}
  =
  -\frac1{4g^2}F^a_{\mu\nu}F^{a\mu\nu},
  \qquad
  F^a_{\mu\nu}
  =
  \partial_\mu A^a_\nu-\partial_\nu A^a_\mu
  +f^a{}_{bc}A^b_\mu A^c_\nu .
\]
This is the coupling-absorbed normalization of
Chapter~\ref{ch:vol2-classical-yang-mills-matter}:
\(D_\mu=\partial_\mu-\ii A_\mu\), and the
coupling is the coefficient in front of the curvature term.  In the Abelian
specialization it is related to the explicit-coupling QED convention by
\eqref{eq:ym-qed-curvature-rescaling}.  Thus the beta function extracted below
from the coefficient of \(F^a_{\mu\nu}F^{a\mu\nu}/g^2\) is written in
Yang--Mills field coordinates; QED wavefunction renormalization constants in
Chapter~\ref{ch:qed-renormalization-form-factors} use the explicit-coupling
field coordinate and must first be translated by the same rescaling before
being compared term by term.
The Lorenz gauge condition is
\[
  F^a(A)=\partial^\mu A_\mu^a .
\]
Under the infinitesimal gauge transformation
\[
  \delta_\zeta A^{a\mu}
  =
  \partial^\mu\zeta^a+f^a{}_{bc}A^{b\mu}\zeta^c ,
\]
the variation of \(F^a(A)\) is
\[
  \delta_\zeta F^a(A)
  =
  \partial_\mu
  \left(\partial^\mu\zeta^a+f^a{}_{bc}A^{b\mu}\zeta^c\right).
\]
Replacing the gauge parameter by the ghost \(c^a\), and pairing the gauge
condition with the antighost \(\bar c^a\), gives the Faddeev--Popov action
\begin{equation}
  S_{\mathrm{gh}}
  =
  -\int\dd^4x\,
  \bar c^a\partial_\mu
  \left(\partial^\mu c^a+f^a{}_{bc}A^{b\mu}c^c\right).
  \label{eq:qcd-ordinary-ghost-action}
\end{equation}
Equivalently, after integrating by parts and discarding the boundary term,
\[
  S_{\mathrm{gh}}
  =
  \int\dd^4x\,
  \partial_\mu\bar c^a
  \left(\partial^\mu c^a+f^a{}_{bc}A^{b\mu}c^c\right).
\]
The operator is the massless scalar kinetic operator, while the fields are
Grassmann odd.  With the momentum convention displayed in the figure below,
\begin{equation}
  \bra{\vac}
  \operatorname T\,\widehat{\bar c}^{\,a}(x)\widehat c_b(y)
  \ket{\vac}
  =
  \delta^a{}_{b}
  \int\frac{\dd^4k}{(2\pi)^4}
  \ee^{\ii k\cdot(x-y)}
  \frac{-\ii}{k^2-\ii\epsilon}.
  \label{eq:qcd-ordinary-ghost-propagator}
\end{equation}
The extra sign of a closed ghost loop is a Berezin-Gaussian fact, not an
independent rule.  Let \(K\) denote the free ghost kinetic operator and let
\(V\) denote any matrix of background insertions carrying the ghost-gluon
vertices.  In a finite-dimensional regulator, with the ordered Berezin
convention used for Grassmann Gaussians in the spinor path-integral chapter,
\[
  \frac{Z_{\rm gh}[V]}{Z_{\rm gh}[0]}
  =
  \frac{\int[\dd\bar c\,\dd c]\,
    \exp[-\bar c(K+V)c]}
       {\int[\dd\bar c\,\dd c]\,
    \exp[-\bar cKc]}
  =
  \det(1+K^{-1}V).
\]
Hence the connected \(n\)-insertion ghost cycle is the term
\[
  \operatorname{Tr}\log(1+K^{-1}V)
  =
  \sum_{n\ge1}\frac{(-1)^{n+1}}{n}
  \operatorname{Tr}\bigl((K^{-1}V)^n\bigr).
\]
A commuting complex scalar with the same quadratic operator would instead
produce \(\det(1+K^{-1}V)^{-1}\), and therefore the opposite sign for the same
closed cycle after the local vertex factors are held fixed.  Equivalently, in
the Wick-contraction expansion, closing a ghost cycle requires a cyclic
permutation of Grassmann-odd variables before the \(c\)'s and \(\bar c\)'s are
paired; this cyclic parity is the same closed-loop sign as for fermions.  The
Feynman rules below implement this determinant sign by assigning a factor
\(-1\) to each closed ghost loop.

The ghost-gluon vertex is read from the term
\[
  \int\dd^4x\,
  \partial_\mu\bar c^a\,f^a{}_{bc}A^{b\mu}c^c .
\]
If the momentum \(k\) is assigned to the antighost line as in
figure~\ref{fig:qcd-ordinary-covariant-gauge-rules}, the displayed local
vertex factor is
\begin{equation}
  V_{\bar c^a c^c A^{b\mu}}(k)
  =
  -k_\mu f^a{}_{bc}.
  \label{eq:qcd-ordinary-ghost-gluon-vertex}
\end{equation}
Changing the direction of the ghost arrow or the sign convention for incoming
momenta changes the displayed sign in the corresponding way; the invariant
object is the action \eqref{eq:qcd-ordinary-ghost-action}.  There is no local
\(\bar c\,c\,A\,A\) vertex in this Lorenz gauge, because
\(\partial^\mu D_\mu(A)\) is only linear in \(A_\mu\).

The \(B\)-field form of the gauge-fixing term is
\[
  \int\dd^4x\,
  \left(
    B^a\partial^\mu A_\mu^a+\frac{g^2\xi}{2}B^aB^a
  \right).
\]
Eliminating \(B^a\) gives
\begin{equation}
  S_{\mathrm{gf}}
  =
  -\frac1{2g^2\xi}
  \int\dd^4x\,
  (\partial^\mu A_\mu^a)(\partial^\nu A_\nu^a).
  \label{eq:qcd-ordinary-covariant-gauge-term}
\end{equation}
The quadratic gauge-field action is then inverted by
\begin{equation}
  \bra{\vac}
  \operatorname T\,\widehat A_{a\mu}(x)\widehat A_{b\nu}(y)
  \ket{\vac}
  =
  \int\frac{\dd^4k}{(2\pi)^4}
  \ee^{\ii k\cdot(x-y)}
  g^2
  \frac{-\ii\delta_{ab}}{k^2-\ii\epsilon}
  \left[
    \eta_{\mu\nu}
    +(\xi-1)\frac{k_\mu k_\nu}{k^2}
  \right].
  \label{eq:qcd-ordinary-gluon-propagator}
\end{equation}
The factor \(g^2\) is a consequence of the \(1/g^2\) multiplying the
Yang--Mills kinetic term in the connection normalization used here.

Expanding \(F^a_{\mu\nu}F^{a\mu\nu}\) gives the cubic and quartic gluon
self-interactions:
\begin{align}
  S_{AAA}
  &=
  -\frac1{2g^2}
  \int\dd^4x\,
  f^a{}_{bc}
  \bigl(\partial_\mu A^a_\nu-\partial_\nu A^a_\mu\bigr)
  A^{b\mu} A^{c\nu} ,
  \notag\\
  S_{AAAA}
  &=
  -\frac1{4g^2}
  \int\dd^4x\,
  f^a{}_{bc}f^a{}_{de}
  A^{b\mu}A^{c\nu}A^d_{\mu}A^e_{\nu}.
  \label{eq:qcd-ordinary-gluon-self-interactions}
\end{align}
These displayed action terms specify the convention before any momentum-flow
choice is made for a vertex tensor.  Figure~\ref{fig:qcd-ordinary-covariant-gauge-rules}
records the corresponding propagator and vertex conventions in a notation that
distinguishes quark, ghost, and gauge lines, and also distinguishes present
from absent local vertices; the visual distinction is used below when the
ghost-loop sign and the absent four-ghost vertex are discussed.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \resizebox{0.98\textwidth}{!}{%
  \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1cm,y=1cm,every node/.style={font=\scriptsize},
    ghost/.style={densely dashed,line width=0.75pt},
    gluon/.style={decorate,decoration={snake,amplitude=1.2pt,segment length=5pt},
      line width=0.75pt}]
    \node[font=\small] at (0,3.35)
      {ordinary Lorenz-gauge Yang--Mills rules for Green functions};

    \begin{scope}[shift={(-5.35,1.45)}]

      \node[left] at (-1.45,0) {\(\bar c^a\)};
      \node[right] at (1.45,0) {\(c_b\)};
      \node[blue!70!black] at (0,0.58) {ghost propagator};
      \node[align=center] at (0,-0.68)
        {\(\displaystyle
        \frac{-\ii\delta^a{}_{b}}{k^2-\ii\epsilon}\)};
    \end{scope}

      \draw[gluon] (v)--(0,1.28);

      \draw[red!75!black,line width=1pt] (-0.58,0.78)--(0.58,-0.42);
      \node[align=center] at (0,-0.88)
        {no local \(\bar c c A A\)\\vertex in Lorenz gauge};
    \end{scope}

    \end{scope}

        \draw[gluon] (v)--++(\ang:1.08);

    \end{scope}

  \end{tikzpicture}
  }
  \caption{The ordinary covariant-gauge rules following from the Lorenz
  Faddeev--Popov action.  Dashed arrows denote the ghost-number flow from
  antighost to ghost; wavy lines denote gauge fields.  The rules organize
  gauge-fixed Green functions, while physical state spaces are described by
  BRST cohomology.}
  \label{fig:qcd-ordinary-covariant-gauge-rules}
\end{figure}

\section{Background-Field Derivation of the One-Loop Beta Function}

\subsection{Background Gauge Datum and Effective Coupling Coordinate}
\label{subsec:qcd-background-gauge-effective-coupling}

The coupling in a nonabelian gauge theory is most cleanly extracted from the
coefficient of the background curvature term in the 1PI effective action.  Let
\(A_\mu=\widetilde A_\mu+a_\mu\), where \(\widetilde A_\mu\) is a background
connection and \(a_\mu\) is the quantum fluctuation.  The background covariant
derivative in a representation \(R\) is denoted
\[
  \widetilde D_\mu=\partial_\mu-\ii \widetilde A_\mu^a t_R^a ,
  \qquad
  \widetilde F_{\mu\nu}
  =
  \partial_\mu\widetilde A_\nu-\partial_\nu\widetilde A_\mu
  -\ii[\widetilde A_\mu,\widetilde A_\nu].
\]
In the adjoint representation,
\[
  (\widetilde D_\mu X)^a
  =
  \partial_\mu X^a+f^a{}_{bc}\widetilde A_\mu^bX^c .
\]
The structure constants and trace invariants in this calculation are those of
\eqref{eq:qcd-casimir-definitions}; in particular, for fundamental quarks of
\(SU(N_c)\) in the monograph's trace normalization,
\[
  C_A=2N_c,\qquad T_F=1,\qquad C_F=\frac{N_c^2-1}{N_c}.
\]
Choose the background-field gauge condition
\[
  \widetilde D^\mu a_\mu=0 .
\]
Under a background gauge transformation with parameter
\(\zeta=\zeta^a t^a\),
\[
  \delta \widetilde A_\mu=\widetilde D_\mu\zeta,\qquad
  \delta a_\mu=-\ii[a_\mu,\zeta],
\]
and the ghosts and quarks transform in the adjoint and \(R\) representations,
respectively.  Since this transformation acts linearly on the variables
integrated over in the path integral and as a connection transformation on
\(\widetilde A\), the resulting 1PI functional
\(\Gamma[\widetilde A]\) is invariant under background gauge transformations.
Its dimension-four term therefore has the form
\begin{equation}
  \Gamma[\widetilde A]
  \supset
  -\frac1{4g^2(\mu)}
  \int\dd^4x\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}
  \label{eq:qcd-background-effective-coupling}
\end{equation}
in Lorentzian signature, up to terms with more powers of
\(\widetilde F\), covariant derivatives, or quark fields.  The problem is
therefore reduced to computing one coefficient in a gauge-invariant
functional, rather than separately renormalizing gauge-fixed vertices.

Figure~\ref{fig:qcd-background-field-beta} displays the actual coefficient
extraction used below.  The useful object is not a separate renormalization of
each gauge-fixed vertex, but the determinant-weighted coefficient of
\(\int\widetilde F_{\mu\nu}^a\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}\) in the background
effective action.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \resizebox{0.98\textwidth}{!}{%
  \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1cm,y=1cm,every node/.style={font=\scriptsize},
    gluon/.style={decorate,decoration={snake,amplitude=1.2pt,segment length=5pt},
      line width=0.75pt},
    ghost/.style={densely dashed,line width=0.75pt},
    fermion/.style={line width=0.75pt}]
    \node[font=\small] at (0,3.65)
      {background-field one-loop coefficients};

    \begin{scope}[shift={(-5.85,1.18)}]
      \node[align=center] at (0,1.75)
        {external background\\fields \(\widetilde A\)};

      \draw[gluon] (-1.65,0.20)--(-0.80,0.20);
      \draw[gluon] (0.80,0.20)--(1.65,0.20);
      \node[left] at (-1.70,0.20) {\(\widetilde A\)};
      \node[right] at (1.70,0.20) {\(\widetilde A\)};
      \draw[gluon] (0,0.20) circle (0.80);
      \node at (0,0.20) {\(a_\mu\)};

      \draw[gluon] (-1.65,-1.05)--(-0.80,-1.05);
      \draw[gluon] (0.80,-1.05)--(1.65,-1.05);
      \node[left] at (-1.70,-1.05) {\(\widetilde A\)};
      \node[right] at (1.70,-1.05) {\(\widetilde A\)};
      \draw[ghost] (0,-1.05) circle (0.80);
      \draw[-{Latex[length=1.6mm]},ghost] (-0.55,-0.47) arc (135:45:0.78);
      \node at (0,-1.05) {\(c\)};

      \draw[gluon] (-1.65,-2.30)--(-0.80,-2.30);
      \draw[gluon] (0.80,-2.30)--(1.65,-2.30);
      \node[left] at (-1.70,-2.30) {\(\widetilde A\)};
      \node[right] at (1.70,-2.30) {\(\widetilde A\)};
      \draw[fermion,-{Latex[length=1.6mm]}] (0,-2.30) circle (0.80);
      \node at (0,-2.30) {\(q\)};
    \end{scope}

    \draw[thick,rounded corners=2pt,fill=gray!6] (-3.45,2.38) rectangle (6.15,-2.50);
    \draw[thick] (-3.45,1.55)--(6.15,1.55);
    \draw[thick] (-3.45,0.30)--(6.15,0.30);
    \draw[thick] (-3.45,-0.95)--(6.15,-0.95);
    \draw[thick] (-3.45,-1.75)--(6.15,-1.75);
    \draw[thick] (-0.30,2.38)--(-0.30,-2.50);
    \draw[thick] (3.05,2.38)--(3.05,-2.50);

    \node[font=\scriptsize\bfseries] at (-1.90,1.96) {determinant weight};
    \node[font=\scriptsize\bfseries] at (1.38,1.96) {coefficient in \(b\)};
    \node[font=\scriptsize\bfseries,align=center] at (4.58,1.96)
      {contribution to\\\(\beta_0=4b\)};

    \node[align=center] at (-1.90,0.93)
      {\(-\frac12\operatorname{Tr}\log D_A\)\\vector fluctuation};
    \node[align=center] at (1.38,0.93) {\(+\dfrac56 C_A\)};
    \node[align=center] at (4.58,0.93) {\(+\dfrac{10}{3}C_A\)};

    \node[align=center] at (-1.90,-0.32)
      {\(+\operatorname{Tr}\log D_{\rm gh}\)\\Faddeev--Popov ghosts};
    \node[align=center] at (1.38,-0.32) {\(+\dfrac1{12}C_A\)};
    \node[align=center] at (4.58,-0.32) {\(+\dfrac13 C_A\)};

    \node[align=center] at (-1.90,-1.35)
      {\(+N_f\operatorname{Tr}\log D_\psi\)\\Dirac quarks in \(R\)};
    \node[align=center] at (1.38,-1.35) {\(-\dfrac13T_RN_f\)};
    \node[align=center] at (4.58,-1.35) {\(-\dfrac43T_RN_f\)};

    \node[align=center,font=\scriptsize\bfseries] at (-1.90,-2.12)
      {sum};
    \node[align=center,font=\scriptsize\bfseries] at (1.38,-2.12)
      {\(\displaystyle b=\frac{11}{12}C_A-\frac13T_RN_f\)};
    \node[align=center,font=\scriptsize\bfseries] at (4.58,-2.12)
      {\(\displaystyle \beta_0=\frac{11}{3}C_A-\frac43T_RN_f\)};

    \node[align=center,text width=10.3cm] at (0,-3.18)
      {\(\displaystyle
        \Gamma^{(1)}[\widetilde A]\supset
        \frac{b}{8\pi^2}\frac{\mu^{-\epsilon}}{\epsilon}
        \int\dd^Dx\,\widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}\),
        hence
        \(\displaystyle
        \mu\frac{\dd g}{\dd\mu}
        =-\frac{g^3}{16\pi^2}\beta_0+O(g^5)\).};
  \end{tikzpicture}
  }
  \caption{The background-field method keeps the effective action invariant
  under gauge transformations of \(\widetilde A\), so the one-loop running is
  read from the coefficient of \(\widetilde F^2\).  The table records the
  determinant weights and the coefficient \(b\) used in
  \eqref{eq:qcd-background-b-coefficient}; the usual one-loop coefficient is
  \(\beta_0=4b\).}
  \label{fig:qcd-background-field-beta}
\end{figure}

Set the background quark and ghost fields to zero and use background Feynman
gauge.  The part of the action quadratic in \(a_\mu,q,\bar q,c,\bar c\) has
the form
\begin{equation}
  S^{(2)}
  =
  \int\dd^4x\,
  \left[
    -\frac1{4g_0^2}a^\mu_a(D_A)_{a\mu,b\nu}a^\nu_b
    +\bar q D_\psi q
    +\bar c^aD_{\rm gh}^{ab}c^b
  \right],
  \label{eq:qcd-background-quadratic-action}
\end{equation}
where \(g_0\) is the bare coupling.  The operators are differential operators
whose coefficients are built from \(\widetilde D_\mu\) and
\(\widetilde F_{\mu\nu}\).  In background Feynman gauge their principal
pieces are
\[
  D_\psi=-\gamma^\mu\widetilde D_\mu,\qquad
  D_{\rm gh}=\widetilde D_\mu\widetilde D^\mu,
\]
and \(D_A\) is the adjoint vector operator obtained from the quadratic part of
\((\widetilde F+\widetilde D a-\widetilde D a+[a,a])^2\) together with
the gauge-fixing term.  The Gaussian integral gives
\begin{equation}
  \ii\Gamma^{(1)}[\widetilde A]
  =
  \log
  \left[
    \frac{(\det D_\psi)^{N_f}\det D_{\rm gh}}
    {(\det D_A)^{1/2}}
  \right].
  \label{eq:qcd-background-determinant-ratio}
\end{equation}
The numerator reflects Grassmann integration for quarks and ghosts; the
denominator is the square root from the bosonic gauge-field Gaussian.

\subsection{Constant-Background Extraction of the Logarithmic Coefficient}
\label{subsec:qcd-constant-background-log-extraction}

To extract the logarithmic coefficient, take \(\widetilde A_\mu\) to be
approximately constant inside a large box and to vanish smoothly outside.
The box size supplies an infrared scale \(L\sim\mu^{-1}\).  In the constant
region, the functional trace of a function of a differential operator reduces
to an ordinary momentum integral.  Figure~\ref{fig:qcd-constant-background-box}
separates the position-space infrared regulator from the momentum-space
large-\(|k|\) tail whose logarithm gives the ultraviolet pole:

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \resizebox{0.88\textwidth}{!}{%
  \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1cm,y=1cm,every node/.style={font=\scriptsize}]
    \begin{scope}[shift={(-2.65,0)}]
      \draw[thick] (-2.25,-1.35) rectangle (2.25,1.35);
      \fill[blue!10] (-0.85,0) circle (1.02);
      \draw[blue!65!black,thick] (-0.85,0) circle (1.02);
      \node[blue!65!black,align=center] at (-0.85,0)
      {\(\widetilde A_\mu\simeq\text{const.}\)};
      \node[align=center] at (1.05,0.58)
        {\(\widetilde A_\mu\to0\)\\near the boundary};
      \draw[->,thick] (0.98,0.20)--(0.08,0.28);
      \draw[<->,purple!75!black,thick] (-2.25,-1.68)--(2.25,-1.68);
      \node[purple!75!black,below] at (0,-1.68)
        {\(L\sim\mu^{-1}\)};
      \node[align=center] at (0,-2.35) {position-space\\infrared regulator};
    \end{scope}

    \begin{scope}[shift={(3.05,0)}]
      \draw[->,thick] (-1.75,0)--(1.95,0) node[right] {\(k_1\)};
      \draw[->,thick] (0,-1.55)--(0,1.75) node[above] {\(k_2\)};
      \draw[densely dashed,line width=0.85pt] (0,0) circle (0.62);
      \node[below right] at (0.45,-0.44) {\(|k|=\mu\)};
      \draw[orange!85!black,line width=1.25pt,->] (0.78,0.58)
        arc[start angle=37,end angle=83,radius=0.98];
      \draw[orange!85!black,line width=1.25pt,->] (-0.92,-0.72)
        arc[start angle=218,end angle=168,radius=1.18];
      \draw[orange!85!black,line width=1.25pt,->] (1.05,-1.00)--(1.65,-1.42);
      \node[orange!85!black,align=center] at (0.95,1.16)
        {large-\(|k|\)\\tail};
      \node[orange!85!black] at (1.92,-1.48) {\(k\to\infty\)};
      \node[align=center] at (0,-2.35) {momentum-space\\UV logarithm};
    \end{scope}
  \end{tikzpicture}
  }
  \caption{The constant-background computation is a local extraction of the
  logarithmic coefficient.  The background is chosen nearly constant in a
  region of size \(L\), and the scale \(\mu\sim L^{-1}\) is used as the
  infrared cutoff in the momentum integral; the ultraviolet pole is the
  large-momentum tail of the same local trace.}
  \label{fig:qcd-constant-background-box}
\end{figure}

\begin{equation}
  \operatorname{Tr} f(D)
  =
  \int\dd^Dx
  \int\frac{\dd^Dk}{(2\pi)^D}\,
  \operatorname{tr} f(M(k)).
  \label{eq:qcd-constant-background-functional-trace}
\end{equation}
Here \(\operatorname{Tr}\) includes integration over spacetime and trace over
all indices, while \(\operatorname{tr}\) is the finite-dimensional trace over
spin, Lorentz, and color indices of the symbol \(M(k)\).

The coefficient can be extracted either by expanding the finite-dimensional
symbols in \eqref{eq:qcd-constant-background-functional-trace} or,
equivalently, by computing the logarithmic heat-kernel coefficient of the
same local operators.  The equivalence follows because both calculations
isolate the parity-even curvature-squared functional that renormalizes
\(1/g^2\).  In the symbol expansion, the only tensor average needed at fourth
order is
\begin{equation}
  \frac{k^{\nu_1}k^{\nu_2}k^{\nu_3}k^{\nu_4}}{(k^2)^4}
  \longrightarrow
  \frac1{(k^2)^2D(D+2)}
  \left(
    \delta^{\nu_1\nu_2}\delta^{\nu_3\nu_4}
    +\delta^{\nu_1\nu_3}\delta^{\nu_2\nu_4}
    +\delta^{\nu_1\nu_4}\delta^{\nu_2\nu_3}
  \right).
  \label{eq:qcd-k-four-rotational-average}
\end{equation}
The scalar integral carrying the logarithmic divergence is
\begin{equation}
  I_\epsilon(\mu)
  :=
  \int_{|k|>\mu}
  \frac{\dd^{4-\epsilon}k}{(2\pi)^{4-\epsilon}}\frac1{(k^2)^2}
  =
  \frac1{8\pi^2}\frac{\mu^{-\epsilon}}{\epsilon}
  +\hbox{finite}.
  \label{eq:qcd-basic-log-integral}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Heat-Kernel Determinant Calculation}
\label{subsec:qcd-heat-kernel-determinant-calculation}

For a self-contained evaluation, use the Euclidean logarithmic coefficient of
a Laplace-type operator
\[
  \mathcal L=-\nabla^2+E
\]
on a vector bundle \(\mathcal E\).  Let
\(\Omega_{\mu\nu}:=[\nabla_\mu,\nabla_\nu]\).  In flat space,
\begin{equation}
  \operatorname{Tr}\log\mathcal L
  \supset
  -I_\epsilon(\mu)
  \int\dd^Dx\,
  \operatorname{tr}_{\mathcal E}
  \left(
    \frac12 E^2+\frac1{12}\Omega_{\mu\nu}\Omega^{\mu\nu}
  \right).
  \label{eq:qcd-laplace-heat-coefficient}
\end{equation}
This is obtained from
\(\operatorname{Tr}\log\mathcal L=-\int_0^\infty
\frac{\dd t}{t}\operatorname{Tr}\ee^{-t\mathcal L}\) and the flat-space heat
kernel
\[
  \operatorname{Tr}\ee^{-t\mathcal L}
  \sim
  (4\pi t)^{-D/2}
  \int\dd^Dx\,\operatorname{tr}_{\mathcal E}
  \left[
    1+tE+t^2
    \left(
      \frac12 E^2+\frac1{12}\Omega_{\mu\nu}\Omega^{\mu\nu}
    \right)+\cdots
  \right].
\]
The pole in dimensional regularization is the same pole as
\eqref{eq:qcd-basic-log-integral}.

Let \(t_{\rm ad}^a\) denote the Hermitian adjoint-representation generators,
defined by
\[
  (t_{\rm ad}^a)^b{}_{c}=\ii f^b{}_{ac},
  \qquad
  \operatorname{tr}_{\rm ad}(t_{\rm ad}^a t_{\rm ad}^b)
  =
  C_A\delta^{ab}.
\]
The curvature endomorphism of the adjoint covariant derivative is the
anti-Hermitian operator
\[
  \operatorname{ad}\widetilde F_{\mu\nu}
  :=
  -\ii \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}t_{\rm ad}^a .
\]
Thus the adjoint trace used in the heat-kernel coefficient satisfies
\begin{equation}
  \operatorname{tr}_{\rm ad}
  \bigl(
    \operatorname{ad}\widetilde F_{\mu\nu}\,
    \operatorname{ad}\widetilde F^{\mu\nu}
  \bigr)
  =
  (-\ii)^2
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{b\mu\nu}
  \operatorname{tr}_{\rm ad}(t_{\rm ad}^a t_{\rm ad}^b)
  =
  -C_A\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu},
  \label{eq:qcd-adjoint-trace-sign}
\end{equation}
where the sign comes from the two explicit factors of \(-\ii\).  For a matter
representation \(R\) with Hermitian generators \(t_R^a\),
\[
  \operatorname{tr}_{R}
  \bigl(
    (-\ii \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}t_R^a)
    (-\ii \widetilde F^{b\mu\nu}t_R^b)
  \bigr)
  =
  -T_R\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}.
\]

First consider one Dirac quark in \(R\).  The first-order operator is
\(\mathcal D_\psi:=\gamma^\mu\widetilde D_\mu\), and the logarithmic
part of \(\operatorname{Tr}\log\mathcal D_\psi\) is one half of that
of the Laplace-type operator \(-\mathcal D_\psi^{\,2}\).  With
\[
  -\mathcal D_\psi^{\,2}
  =
  -\widetilde D^2
  -\frac12\gamma^{\mu\nu}\Omega_{\mu\nu},
  \qquad
  \gamma^{\mu\nu}:=\frac12[\gamma^\mu,\gamma^\nu],
\]
one has
\[
  E_\psi=-\frac12\gamma^{\mu\nu}\Omega_{\mu\nu}.
\]
Using
\[
  \operatorname{tr}_{\rm spin}
  \gamma^{\mu\nu}\gamma^{\rho\sigma}
  =
  4\left(
    \delta^{\mu\sigma}\delta^{\nu\rho}
    -\delta^{\mu\rho}\delta^{\nu\sigma}
  \right),
\]
the heat-kernel density is
\[
  \operatorname{tr}_{\rm spin\otimes R}
  \left(
    \frac12E_\psi^2
    +\frac1{12}\Omega_{\mu\nu}\Omega^{\mu\nu}
  \right)
  =
  \frac23 T_R\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}.
\]
The extra factor \(1/2\) relating
\(\operatorname{Tr}\log\mathcal D_\psi\) to
\(\operatorname{Tr}\log(-\mathcal D_\psi^{\,2})\) gives
\begin{equation}
  \operatorname{Tr}\log D_\psi
  \supset
  -\frac13 T_R I_\epsilon(\mu)
  \int\dd^Dx\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}
  \label{eq:qcd-quark-determinant-f2}
\end{equation}
for one Dirac flavor.

For the Faddeev--Popov ghosts in background gauge, the relevant Laplace-type
operator is the scalar adjoint operator \(\mathcal L_{\rm gh}=-\widetilde
D^2\).  Hence \(E_{\rm gh}=0\), and
\eqref{eq:qcd-laplace-heat-coefficient} gives
\begin{equation}
  \operatorname{Tr}\log D_{\rm gh}
  \supset
  \frac1{12}C_A I_\epsilon(\mu)
  \int\dd^Dx\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}.
  \label{eq:qcd-ghost-determinant-f2}
\end{equation}

For the gauge fluctuation in background Feynman gauge, the Euclidean vector
operator has the form
\[
  (\mathcal L_A)_\mu{}^\nu
  =
  -\widetilde D^2\delta_\mu{}^\nu
  -2\,\operatorname{ad}(\widetilde F_\mu{}^\nu).
\]
It is a Laplace-type operator on one-forms valued in the adjoint bundle with
\[
  (E_A)_\mu{}^\nu=-2\,\operatorname{ad}(\widetilde F_\mu{}^\nu),
  \qquad
  (\Omega_{\rho\sigma})_\mu{}^\nu
  =
  \delta_\mu{}^\nu\,\operatorname{ad}(\widetilde F_{\rho\sigma}).
\]
The finite-dimensional trace is taken in \(D=4-\epsilon\) one-form
components and in the adjoint representation.  Using
\eqref{eq:qcd-adjoint-trace-sign} and the antisymmetry of
\(\widetilde F_{\mu\nu}\),
\begin{align}
  \operatorname{tr}\frac12E_A^2
  &=
  \frac12\operatorname{tr}_{\rm ad}
  \left[
    (-2\operatorname{ad}\widetilde F_\mu{}^\nu)
    (-2\operatorname{ad}\widetilde F_\nu{}^\mu)
  \right]
  =
  2C_A\,\widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu},
  \label{eq:qcd-vector-e-trace}\\
  \operatorname{tr}\frac1{12}\Omega_{\rho\sigma}\Omega^{\rho\sigma}
  &=
  \frac{D}{12}\operatorname{tr}_{\rm ad}
  \bigl(
    \operatorname{ad}\widetilde F_{\rho\sigma}
    \operatorname{ad}\widetilde F^{\rho\sigma}
  \bigr)
  =
  -\frac{D}{12}C_A\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\rho\sigma}\widetilde F^{a\rho\sigma}.
  \label{eq:qcd-vector-omega-trace-d}
\end{align}
The \(D\)-dependence in \eqref{eq:qcd-vector-omega-trace-d} has an
evanescent part.  Write
\[
  \mu^{-\epsilon}=1-\epsilon L_\mu+O(\epsilon^2),
\]
where \(L_\mu\) is the finite logarithm determined by the subtraction
convention in \eqref{eq:qcd-basic-log-integral}.  Then
\[
  (2-D)\frac{\mu^{-\epsilon}}{\epsilon}
  =
  (-2+\epsilon)\frac{\mu^{-\epsilon}}{\epsilon}
  =
  -\frac2\epsilon+1+2L_\mu+O(\epsilon),
  \qquad
  D\frac{\mu^{-\epsilon}}{\epsilon}
  =
  \frac4\epsilon-1-4L_\mu+O(\epsilon).
\]
Consequently, replacing \(D\) by \(4\) is legitimate for the pole
coefficient; the omitted \((D-4)I_\epsilon(\mu)\) part is a finite local
contribution.
Equations \eqref{eq:qcd-vector-e-trace} and
\eqref{eq:qcd-vector-omega-trace-d} therefore give the pole density
\[
  \left(
    2-\frac{4}{12}
  \right)C_A\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}
  =
  \frac53C_A\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}.
\]
The vector determinant contribution is therefore
\begin{equation}
  \operatorname{Tr}\log D_A
  \supset
  -\frac53 C_A I_\epsilon(\mu)
  \int\dd^Dx\,
  \widetilde F^a_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{a\mu\nu}.
  \label{eq:qcd-vector-determinant-f2}
\end{equation}

\subsection{One-Loop Coupling Flow}
\label{subsec:qcd-background-one-loop-coupling-flow}

Combining \eqref{eq:qcd-quark-determinant-f2},
\eqref{eq:qcd-ghost-determinant-f2}, and
\eqref{eq:qcd-vector-determinant-f2} with the determinant weights in
\eqref{eq:qcd-background-determinant-ratio} gives the coefficient
\begin{equation}
  b
  =
  -\frac{N_f}{3}T_R
  +
  \left[
    -\frac12\left(-\frac53\right)+\frac1{12}
  \right]C_A
  =
  \frac{11}{12}C_A-\frac{N_f}{3}T_R .
  \label{eq:qcd-background-b-coefficient}
\end{equation}
The term \(5C_A/6\) comes from the vector determinant after the bosonic
factor \(-1/2\), the term \(C_A/12\) from the Faddeev--Popov determinant, and
the term \(-N_fT_R/3\) from the \(N_f\) quark determinants.

Comparing the logarithmic part of \(\Gamma[\widetilde A]\) with
\eqref{eq:qcd-background-effective-coupling} gives
\begin{equation}
  -\frac1{4g^2(\mu)}
  =
  -\frac1{4g_0^2}
  +
  \frac{b}{8\pi^2}\frac{\mu^{-\epsilon}}{\epsilon}
  +\hbox{finite}.
  \label{eq:qcd-background-bare-renormalized-relation}
\end{equation}
In \(D=4-\epsilon\), write the dimensionless coupling as
\[
  \lambda^2(\mu)=\mu^\epsilon g^2(\mu).
\]
This is the gauge-theory analogue of the scalar convention
\(g^\epsilon=\mu^\epsilon[\lambda+\cdots]\): the square appears because the
Yang--Mills kinetic term is parameterized by \(1/g^2\).  Thus \(\lambda\) is
the dimensionless \(D\)-dimensional coupling coordinate, while \(g(\mu)\)
retains the engineering dimension \(-\epsilon/2\).
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
  \frac1{g_0^2}
  =
  \mu^\epsilon
  \left[
    \frac1{\lambda^2(\mu)}
    +
    \frac{b}{2\pi^2}\frac1{\epsilon}
    +\hbox{finite}
    +O(\lambda^2)
  \right].
  \label{eq:qcd-background-bare-coupling-ms}
\end{equation}
The bare coupling \(g_0\) is independent of the auxiliary scale \(\mu\).
Differentiating \eqref{eq:qcd-background-bare-coupling-ms} at fixed \(g_0\)
and then taking \(\epsilon\to0\) gives
\begin{equation}
  \frac{\dd}{\dd\log\mu}\frac1{\lambda^2(\mu)}
  =
  \frac{b}{2\pi^2}+O(\lambda^2),
  \qquad
  \beta(\lambda)
  =
  -\frac{b}{4\pi^2}\lambda^3+O(\lambda^5).
  \label{eq:qcd-background-beta-lambda}
\end{equation}
Setting \(\epsilon=0\) and writing \(g\) for the four-dimensional
dimensionless coupling,
\[
  \frac{b}{4\pi^2}
  =
  \frac1{16\pi^2}
  \left(
    \frac{11}{3}C_A-\frac{4}{3}T_RN_f
  \right),
\]
which is \eqref{eq:qcd-one-loop-beta-general}.  For fundamental quarks of
\(SU(N_c)\), \(T_R=T_F=1\) and \(C_A=2N_c\), giving
\eqref{eq:qcd-one-loop-beta-fundamental}.

\section{Dimensional Transmutation}

Assume
\[
  b_0:=11N_c-2N_f>0 .
\]
At one loop,
\[
  \mu\frac{\dd g}{\dd\mu}
  =
  -\frac{b_0}{24\pi^2}g^3 .
\]
Integrating between \(\mu_0\) and \(\mu\) gives
\begin{equation}
  \frac1{g^2(\mu)}
  =
  \frac1{g^2(\mu_0)}
  +
  \frac{b_0}{12\pi^2}\log\frac{\mu}{\mu_0}.
  \label{eq:qcd-one-loop-running}
\end{equation}
Thus \(g(\mu)\) decreases at large \(\mu\).  This property is asymptotic
freedom.

The integration constant may be traded for a scale.  At one-loop accuracy,
\begin{equation}
  \Lambda
  =
  \mu\,
  \exp\left\{
  -\frac{12\pi^2}{b_0 g^2(\mu)}
  \right\}.
  \label{eq:qcd-lambda-one-loop}
\end{equation}
Higher-loop definitions multiply the right side by powers and series in
\(g(\mu)\), and the numerical value depends on the renormalization scheme.
The scheme-independent statement is that a dimensionless renormalized coupling
has been replaced, after a renormalization condition is chosen, by a
dimensionful RG-invariant scale, together with the quark masses.  This is
dimensional transmutation.

Perturbative predictions at external momentum scale \(Q\) are organized by
the small parameter \(g(Q)\) when \(Q\gg\Lambda\).  At scales comparable to
\(\Lambda\), the perturbative expansion is no longer a controlled expansion in
small coupling.  Statements about the spectrum, confinement, and the mass gap
then require nonperturbative input.

\begin{remark}[Mass-gap problem and the status of continuum Yang--Mills]
\label{rem:qcd-mass-gap-status}
For pure compact \(SU(N_c)\) Yang--Mills theory in four dimensions, the
existence of the continuum quantum field theory on \(\mathbb R^4\) and the
existence of a positive mass gap form the Yang--Mills Millennium problem; see
Remark~\ref{rem:four-dimensional-yang-mills-status}.  In this monograph the
pure-\(SU(N_c)\) continuum, vacuum, mass-gap, confinement, and spectral package
is Conjecture~\ref{conj:pure-yang-mills-central}.  The issue is not the formal
writing of the action or the perturbative construction of renormalized Green
functions.  It is the construction of a nonperturbative continuum measure, or
equivalently a Hilbert-space or algebraic quantum theory, whose correlation
functions satisfy the required structural properties and whose physical
spectrum above the vacuum has a strictly positive lower bound.

The finite-lattice theory at nonzero lattice spacing is a well-defined
statistical-mechanical system, and numerical as well as analytic evidence
strongly supports a gapped pure Yang--Mills continuum limit.  The proof of
that continuum limit and its gap in \(D=4\) remains a separate mathematical
problem.  The QCD discussion below therefore treats confinement, flux tubes,
and hadronic spectral statements as nonperturbative physical inputs or as
properties of regulated/lattice formulations, not as consequences of the
one-loop beta function.
\end{remark}

\section{Regulated Gauge-Invariant Matrix Elements}
\label{sec:qcd-regulated-gauge-invariant-matrix-elements}

The basic nonperturbative quantities of QCD are not matrix elements of isolated
colored fields.  They are limits, when such limits exist, of regulated
gauge-invariant matrix elements.  A calculation may use gauge-fixed quark and
gluon variables internally, but the object being defined must be a
color-singlet operator, a line operator, or a BRST-cohomology class with a
specified regulator and renormalization prescription.

\begin{definition}[Regulated gauge-invariant QCD matrix-element specification]
\label{def:qcd-regulated-matrix-element-specification}
A regulated gauge-invariant QCD matrix-element specification consists of:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item a regulator family \(\mathcal R_\epsilon\), such as a finite lattice,
  a gauge-covariant heat-kernel cutoff, or a gauge-fixed BV regulator together
  with its quantum master equation;
  \item a regulated operator \(\mathcal O_\epsilon\) built from local
  gauge-invariant fields, closed Wilson loops, or colored fields whose exposed
  color indices are joined by Wilson transporters along specified paths;
  \item the external states or density matrix in which the operator is
  evaluated, for example hadron states, the vacuum, a thermal state, or a
  finite-volume transfer-matrix state;
  \item ultraviolet, endpoint, cusp, and rapidity subtractions whenever the
  operator contains coincident fields, cusped Wilson lines, infinite Wilson
  lines, or lightlike separations;
  \item a topology in which the renormalized matrix elements are required to
  converge as \(\epsilon\to0\), usually a distribution topology in spacetime,
  momentum fraction, or detector angle.
\end{enumerate}
Only after these data have been supplied does a symbol such as a Wilson loop,
PDF, fragmentation function, soft function, quasi-PDF, static potential, or
energy-flow correlator denote a nonperturbative QCD quantity.  If the
continuum limit or lightcone limit is not known to exist, the same formula is a
regulated definition plus a continuum-existence hypothesis, not a theorem.
\end{definition}

The Wilson transporter is the mechanism by which separated colored insertions
become gauge-covariant before their endpoint indices are contracted.  For
example,
\[
  \bar q(x)W_{\square}(x,y)q(y),\qquad
  F^a_{\mu\nu}(x)W_{\rm ad}^{ab}(x,y)F^b_{\rho\sigma}(y),
\]
are gauge-invariant bilocals after the spinor and Lorentz indices are
contracted as specified.  Closed Wilson loops are obtained by taking the color
trace of a transporter around a closed curve.  Baryonic static-source
operators join \(N_c\) fundamental Wilson lines at a color-antisymmetric
junction.  Soft functions and parton distributions use Wilson lines whose
directions are fixed by the high-energy kinematics; their lightlike or cusped
nature is a source of additional renormalization, not an optional convention.

This rule is not limited to perturbation theory.  On a lattice, the transporter
is the ordered product of link variables and gauge invariance is exact at
nonzero lattice spacing.  In a continuum path-integral or Hilbert-space
construction, the same Wilson-line operator must be obtained as a renormalized
composite.  Perturbative Feynman graphs then compute short-distance
coefficients, anomalous dimensions, and matching kernels for these
nonperturbative matrix elements.  They do not replace the matrix elements by
colored asymptotic particles.  Energy correlators are a useful complementary
case: the detector operator is built from the gauge-invariant stress tensor,
so no Wilson line is needed in the operator definition, although perturbative
representatives of the same observable still involve colored internal
coordinates after factorization.

\section{Infrared Possibilities and Wilson Loop Diagnostics}

The one-loop beta function determines the ultraviolet direction of the flow
when \(B_0>0\).  It does not by itself determine the infrared theory.  The
first controlled alternative to strong infrared coupling is visible only after
the two-loop term is kept.  Put
\[
  a(\mu):=\frac{g(\mu)^2}{16\pi^2}.
\]
In a mass-independent gauge-invariant renormalization scheme, the two-loop
beta function has the form
\begin{equation}
  \beta_a(a)
  :=
  \mu\frac{\dd a}{\dd\mu}
  =
  -2B_0a^2-2B_1a^3+O(a^4),
  \label{eq:banks-zaks-beta-a-general}
\end{equation}
where the one-loop coefficient is the one derived above,
\[
  B_0=\frac{11}{3}C_A-\frac{4}{3}T_RN_f,
\]
and the two-loop coefficient is
\begin{equation}
  B_1
  =
  \frac{34}{3}C_A^2
  -
  \left(
    \frac{20}{3}C_AT_R+4C_RT_R
  \right)N_f .
  \label{eq:banks-zaks-two-loop-coefficient}
\end{equation}
The constants \(C_A,T_R,C_R\) are those of
\eqref{eq:qcd-casimir-definitions}; in particular no hidden
\(\operatorname{tr}(T^aT^b)=\frac12\delta^{ab}\) convention is being used.
The coefficient \(B_1\) is the universal two-loop coefficient of the
mass-independent gauge beta function.  The one-loop derivation in the
preceding section fixes \(B_0\) in the present normalization; the present
subsection uses the two-loop coefficient to derive the fixed point and its
domain of validity.

The universality of \(B_0\) and \(B_1\) is elementary at the level of coupling
coordinates.  Let \(a'\) be another dimensionless coupling coordinate with
\[
  a'=a+r_1a^2+r_2a^3+O(a^4).
\]
Then
\[
  \beta_{a'}(a')
  =
  \frac{\dd a'}{\dd a}\,\beta_a(a)
  =
  -2B_0a'^2-2B_1a'^3+O(a'^4).
\]
Thus a small two-loop zero is not an artifact of a quadratic coordinate
change.  Its precise location beyond the first nontrivial order is
scheme-dependent, because \(O(a^4)\) terms affect the next coefficient in the
small-\(B_0\) expansion.

For fundamental quarks in the trace convention of this monograph,
\[
  C_A=2N_c,\qquad T_F=1,\qquad C_F=\frac{N_c^2-1}{N_c}.
\]
Hence
\begin{equation}
  B_0
  =
  \frac{2}{3}(11N_c-2N_f),
  \qquad
  B_1
  =
  \frac{136}{3}N_c^2
  -
  \left(\frac{52}{3}N_c-\frac4{N_c}\right)N_f .
  \label{eq:banks-zaks-fundamental-B0-B1}
\end{equation}
Asymptotic freedom requires \(B_0>0\), while a perturbative infrared zero
requires \(B_1<0\).  These inequalities give the weakly coupled Banks--Zaks
window
\begin{equation}
  \frac{34N_c^3}{13N_c^2-3}
  <
  N_f
  <
  \frac{11}{2}N_c .
  \label{eq:banks-zaks-window-core}
\end{equation}
inside the larger asymptotically free range \(N_f<11N_c/2\).  More precisely,
define the distance from the upper edge by
\begin{equation}
  \epsilon_{\rm BZ}
  :=
  \frac{11N_c-2N_f}{3},
  \qquad
  N_f=\frac{11}{2}N_c-\frac32\epsilon_{\rm BZ}.
  \label{eq:banks-zaks-epsilon}
\end{equation}
Then \(B_0=2\epsilon_{\rm BZ}\), and
\[
  B_1
  =
  -2(25N_c^2-11)
  +
  \left(26N_c-\frac6{N_c}\right)\epsilon_{\rm BZ}.
\]
The two-loop zero is
\begin{equation}
  a_*^{(2)}
  =
  -\frac{B_0}{B_1}
  =
  \frac{2\epsilon_{\rm BZ}}
       {2(25N_c^2-11)
        -(26N_c-6/N_c)\epsilon_{\rm BZ}},
  \label{eq:banks-zaks-two-loop-zero}
\end{equation}
and the fixed-point coordinate of the full perturbative beta function is
\begin{equation}
  a_*
  =
  \frac{\epsilon_{\rm BZ}}{25N_c^2-11}
  +O(\epsilon_{\rm BZ}^2).
  \label{eq:banks-zaks-leading-fixed-point}
\end{equation}
The \(O(\epsilon_{\rm BZ}^2)\) coefficient is not determined by
\eqref{eq:banks-zaks-beta-a-general} alone, because it receives a contribution
from the three-loop coefficient.  The leading coefficient in
\eqref{eq:banks-zaks-leading-fixed-point}, by contrast, is scheme-independent.

The linearized exponent of the coupling perturbation is obtained without an
extra assumption:
\begin{equation}
  \omega_{\rm BZ}
  :=
  \left.\frac{\partial\beta_a}{\partial a}\right|_{a=a_*}
  =
  -\frac{2B_0^2}{B_1}+O(B_0^3)
  =
  \frac{4\epsilon_{\rm BZ}^2}{25N_c^2-11}
  +O(\epsilon_{\rm BZ}^3).
  \label{eq:banks-zaks-coupling-exponent}
\end{equation}
With the convention \(\beta_a=\dd a/\dd\log\mu\), the positive sign means that
the perturbation \(a-a_*\) decays when the flow is followed toward the
infrared.  The massless theory is essential: quark mass terms are relevant
deformations of the fixed point and must be tuned to zero.  At fixed finite
\(N_c\), \(N_f\) is an integer, so \(\epsilon_{\rm BZ}\) is not continuously
adjustable in the literal theory.  The perturbative expansion may be viewed
as an analytic expansion in the fermion-determinant power \(N_f\), or more
physically in the Veneziano regime with \(N_f/N_c\) just below \(11/2\), where
the 't Hooft coupling \(N_c a_*\) is small.

The fixed points obtained in this way are the Banks--Zaks fixed points.  The
statement is perturbative: it constructs a weak-coupling zero of the
renormalized massless beta function and gives perturbative scaling data for
gauge-invariant local operators.  It is not a derivation of confinement or of
the low-flavor infrared behavior of QCD.

\begin{controlledapproximation}[Banks--Zaks perturbative fixed point versus a conformal-window theorem]
\label{ca:qcd-banks-zaks-status}
The Banks--Zaks construction used here is the following perturbative construction,
not a theorem that every theory with flavor number in
\eqref{eq:banks-zaks-window-core} is a nonperturbative conformal field theory.
One fixes a mass-independent gauge-invariant renormalization scheme, restricts
to the massless theory, treats \(N_f\) near the upper edge of asymptotic
freedom so that \(a_*=O(\epsilon_{\rm BZ})\), and computes renormalized
correlators of gauge-invariant local or light-ray operators as formal
asymptotic series in the small fixed-point coordinate.  The first two beta
coefficients determine the leading location
\eqref{eq:banks-zaks-leading-fixed-point} and the leading irrelevant exponent
\eqref{eq:banks-zaks-coupling-exponent}; higher coefficients and finite
operator normalizations are scheme coordinates.  If a nonperturbative
regulator and scaling limit are supplied and the RG trajectory remains in the
same small-coupling neighborhood, these perturbative data are the local
coordinates of the resulting infrared theory.  Without that extra
construction they are a controlled perturbative fixed-point expansion.  The
lower edge of the nonperturbative conformal window, if it exists for the
chosen gauge group and matter content, is not determined by
\eqref{eq:banks-zaks-beta-a-general}.
\end{controlledapproximation}

Figure~\ref{fig:qcd-banks-zaks-beta-shapes} compares the two beta-function
shapes relevant here: asymptotic freedom without a weak infrared zero and the
Banks--Zaks case with a perturbative infrared fixed point.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \resizebox{0.92\textwidth}{!}{%
  \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1cm,y=1cm,every node/.style={font=\scriptsize}]
    \node[font=\small] at (0,2.45)
      {two infrared possibilities visible in a two-loop beta function};
    \draw[-{Latex[length=2mm]}] (-0.35,0)--(6.4,0) node[below] {\(a\)};
    \draw[-{Latex[length=2mm]}] (0,-1.55)--(0,1.55) node[left] {\(\beta_a(a)\)};
    \draw[blue!70!black,very thick]
      (0,0) .. controls (1.4,-0.22) and (3.1,-0.85) .. (5.7,-1.35);
    \node[blue!70!black,fill=white,inner sep=1.2pt,align=center] at (4.55,-0.98)
      {\(\beta<0\) through\\the perturbative range};
    \draw[purple!75!black,very thick,dash pattern=on 5pt off 2.5pt]
      (0,0) .. controls (0.85,-0.35) and (1.55,-0.42) .. (2.38,0)
      .. controls (3.25,0.48) and (4.25,1.0) .. (5.45,1.24);
    \fill[red!75!black] (2.38,0) circle (2.6pt);
    \node[red!75!black,align=center] at (2.08,0.58)
      {IR fixed\\point};
    \node[purple!75!black,align=center] at (4.35,1.18)
      {Banks--Zaks\\zero};
  \end{tikzpicture}
  }
  \caption{A negative one-loop coefficient implies ultraviolet asymptotic
  freedom.  The infrared behavior depends on the subsequent terms.  The solid
  curve has no weak infrared zero, while the dashed curve has a small two-loop
  zero giving a controlled perturbative fixed point; absence of such a zero
  leaves the flow outside perturbative control in the infrared.}
  \label{fig:qcd-banks-zaks-beta-shapes}
\end{figure}

For smaller \(N_f\), perturbation theory around the ultraviolet fixed point no
longer determines the infrared limit.  The confining regime discussed below is
therefore a conditional infrared scenario, not a conclusion derived from the
one-loop beta function alone.  The condition called confinement here is a
spectral and observable hypothesis: isolated finite-energy asymptotic
particles carry no color, and physical states are created from the vacuum by
gauge-invariant operators.  Deriving this spectral statement from the
four-dimensional continuum QCD Lagrangian is an open nonperturbative problem.
Quark and gluon fields remain part of the short-distance gauge-fixed
description; the confinement statement concerns the gauge-invariant
Hilbert-space spectrum and the algebra of physical observables.

The basic nonlocal observable for this confinement diagnostic is the Wilson
line.  For an oriented path \(C\) from \(x_1\) to \(x_2\), define
\begin{equation}
  W_R(C)
  =
  P\exp\left\{
    \ii\int_C A_\mu^a(x)t_R^a\,\dd x^\mu
  \right\},
  \label{eq:qcd-wilson-line-definition}
\end{equation}
where \(R\) is a representation of the gauge group, \(t_R^a\) are its
generators, and \(P\) denotes ordering along the path.  The nonlocal operator
\[
  \bar q(x_2)\,W_R(C)\,q(x_1)
\]
is gauge invariant when the endpoints transform in conjugate representations.
It creates a color-neutral state containing a quark, an antiquark, and the
gauge flux connecting them.

Figure~\ref{fig:qcd-wilson-line-loop} fixes the Wilson-line convention used for
gauge-invariant quark bilinears and for closed Wilson-loop observables: path
ordering is along the chosen curve, and endpoint representations are conjugate.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \resizebox{0.96\textwidth}{!}{%
  \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1cm,y=1cm,every node/.style={font=\scriptsize}]
    \begin{scope}
      \node[font=\small,align=center] at (0,2.45)
        {open Wilson line};
      \draw[thick] (-2.35,0) .. controls (-1.2,0.65) and (1.05,-0.55) .. (2.35,0.08);
      \foreach \dy/\col in {-0.25/purple!70!black,0/purple!55!black,0.25/purple!70!black} {
        \draw[\col,thick,decorate,decoration={snake,amplitude=0.85pt,segment length=7pt}]
          (-2.15,\dy) .. controls (-1.05,{0.58+\dy}) and (0.95,{-0.50+\dy}) .. (2.15,{0.08+\dy});
      }
      \fill[blue!75!black] (-2.35,0) circle (2.8pt);
      \fill[red!75!black] (2.35,0.08) circle (2.8pt);
      \node[below left,blue!75!black] at (-2.35,0) {quark};
      \node[below right,red!75!black] at (2.35,0.08) {anti-quark};
      \node[above] at (0.15,0.55) {\(C\)};
      \node[purple!75!black,align=center] at (0,-1.02)
        {gauge flux carried by \(W_R(C)\)};
    \end{scope}

    \begin{scope}[shift={(6.2,0)}]
      \node[font=\small,align=center] at (0,2.45)
        {rectangular Wilson loop};
      \draw[->,thick] (-1.7,-1.0)--(1.7,-1.0)--(1.7,1.0)--(-1.7,1.0)--(-1.7,-0.92);
      \draw[<->,blue!70!black,thick] (-1.7,-1.34)--(1.7,-1.34);
      \node[blue!70!black,below] at (0,-1.34) {\(L\)};
      \draw[<->,orange!85!black,thick] (2.05,-1.0)--(2.05,1.0);
      \node[orange!85!black,right] at (2.05,0) {\(T\)};
      \node[align=center] at (0,0)
        {Euclidean time\\evolution of\\static sources};
      \node[purple!75!black,align=center] at (0,-1.95)
        {\(\langle W_R(C)\rangle\sim\ee^{-V(L)T}\)};
    \end{scope}
  \end{tikzpicture}
  }
  \caption{An open Wilson line attached to endpoint fields gives a
  gauge-invariant operator with color flux between the endpoints.  A large
  Euclidean rectangular Wilson loop extracts the static potential between
  external color sources.}
  \label{fig:qcd-wilson-line-loop}
\end{figure}

In pure Yang--Mills theory, where there are no dynamical fundamental quarks,
a fundamental flux string cannot break by pair creation of fundamental matter.
It carries a charge under the center one-form symmetry.  For a large Euclidean
rectangle \(C_{L,T}\) of spatial width \(L\) and time height \(T\),
\begin{equation}
  \langle W_R(C_{L,T})\rangle
  \sim
  \ee^{-V_R(L)T}
  \qquad (T\to\infty).
  \label{eq:qcd-wilson-loop-static-potential}
\end{equation}
An area law
\[
  \langle W_R(C_{L,T})\rangle\sim\ee^{-\gamma LT}
\]
is equivalent, in this static-source setup, to a linearly growing potential
\[
  V_R(L)\sim\gamma L .
\]
Chapter~\ref{ch:lattice-yang-mills-nonperturbative-definition} gives the
finite-cutoff version of this statement: at sufficiently small lattice
\(\beta\), the character expansion of the Wilson action converges and gives an
area bound for fundamental Wilson loops.  That theorem belongs to the
strong-coupling lattice regime.  A proof of the corresponding continuum
four-dimensional pure Yang--Mills area law would require control of the
\(\beta\to\infty\) scaling trajectory and is part of the same
nonperturbative construction problem as the mass gap, as separated in
Conjecture~\ref{conj:pure-yang-mills-central}.
In QCD with dynamical fundamental quarks, the same Wilson loop is no longer a
sharp order parameter for confinement because the flux tube can break by
quark-antiquark pair creation.  The physical spectral statement remains that
asymptotic particles are color singlets.

\section{\texorpdfstring{The 't Hooft Large-\(N_c\) Expansion}{The 't Hooft Large-Nc Expansion}}

\subsection{Planar Counting and Factorization}
\label{subsec:qcd-large-n-planar-counting-factorization}

The large-\(N_c\) expansion is a statement about a sequence of gauge theories,
not about a single theory at \(N_c=3\).  The sequence used here is
\(SU(N_c)\) Yang--Mills theory with the same trace convention as above,
\[
  \operatorname{tr}_{\square}(t^at^b)=\delta^{ab},
  \qquad
  C_A=2N_c,\qquad T_F=1,
\]
and with
\begin{equation}
  \lambda:=g^2N_c
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-lambda-definition}
\end{equation}
held fixed as \(N_c\to\infty\).  In the common half-trace convention
\(\operatorname{tr}(T^aT^b)=\frac12\delta^{ab}\), the corresponding coupling
is \(g_{\rm ht}^2=2g^2\) and the conventional 't Hooft coupling is
\(\lambda_{\rm ht}=g_{\rm ht}^2N_c=2\lambda\).  The constant factor \(2\)
has no effect on the \(N_c\)-power counting, but it matters when comparing
formulae across conventions.

For the color-counting argument it is useful to write the gauge field as a
traceless matrix,
\[
  A_\mu=A_\mu^a t^a,\qquad (A_\mu)^i{}_i=0,
\]
and to separate the color-index contractions from the spacetime integrals,
spin tensors, gauge-fixing tensors, and momentum denominators.  The
fundamental finite-dimensional identity in the present trace normalization is
\begin{equation}
  \sum_{a=1}^{N_c^2-1}
  (t^a)^i{}_{j}(t^a)^k{}_{\ell}
  =
  \delta^i{}_{\ell}\delta^k{}_{j}
  -\frac1{N_c}\delta^i{}_{j}\delta^k{}_{\ell}.
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-su-completeness}
\end{equation}
Here is the derivation, since this is where convention errors most often
enter.  On \(\operatorname{End}(\mathbb C^{N_c})\) use the bilinear pairing
\((X,Y)=\operatorname{tr}_{\square}(XY)\).  The matrix units have completeness
kernel \(\delta^i{}_{\ell}\delta^k{}_{j}\), which is the identity operator on
all matrices.  The one-dimensional scalar subspace is spanned by
\(\mathbf 1\), whose squared norm is \(N_c\), so the projector onto the scalar
subspace has kernel \(N_c^{-1}\delta^i{}_{j}\delta^k{}_{\ell}\).  Since
\(\{t^a\}\) is an orthonormal basis of the traceless subspace, summing
\((t^a)^i{}_j(t^a)^k{}_\ell\) gives the identity projector minus the scalar
projector, which is exactly \eqref{eq:qcd-large-n-su-completeness}.
The first term is the \(U(N_c)\) double-line propagator: one color line carries
the fundamental index and the other carries the antifundamental index.  The
second term subtracts the trace part and is explicitly lower order in
\(1/N_c\).  It is therefore included in the expansion as a subleading
insertion; it cannot increase the leading power of \(N_c\).  Ghost fields in
covariant gauges are adjoint-valued and have the same double-line color
structure, with the Grassmann sign already accounted for by the determinant
argument above.  Quark propagators carry one fundamental color line.

\begin{figure}[H]
  \centering
  \resizebox{0.96\textwidth}{!}{%
  \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1cm,y=1cm,every node/.style={font=\scriptsize}]
    \begin{scope}
      \node[font=\small] at (0,2.25) {adjoint color line};
      \draw[blue!75!black,thick] (-2.0,0.18)--(2.0,0.18);
      \draw[red!75!black,thick] (2.0,-0.18)--(-2.0,-0.18);
      \draw[-{Latex[length=2mm]},blue!75!black] (-0.8,0.42)--(0.3,0.42);
      \draw[-{Latex[length=2mm]},red!75!black] (0.8,-0.42)--(-0.3,-0.42);
      \node[blue!75!black] at (-2.25,0.18) {\(i\)};
      \node[blue!75!black] at (2.25,0.18) {\(\ell\)};
      \node[red!75!black] at (2.25,-0.18) {\(k\)};
      \node[red!75!black] at (-2.25,-0.18) {\(j\)};
      \node[align=center] at (0,-1.05)
        {\(\delta^i{}_{\ell}\delta^k{}_{j}\)\\leading term of
        \eqref{eq:qcd-large-n-su-completeness}};
    \end{scope}

    \begin{scope}[shift={(6.4,0)}]
      \node[font=\small] at (0,2.25) {planar theta graph};
      \coordinate (L) at (-1.45,0);
      \coordinate (R) at (1.45,0);
      \foreach \y/\col in {0.82/blue!65!black,0/purple!70!black,-0.82/red!70!black} {
        \draw[\col,very thick]
          (L) .. controls (-0.65,\y) and (0.65,\y) .. (R);
        \draw[\col,very thick]
          ($(L)+(0,-0.12)$) .. controls (-0.65,{\y-0.12}) and
          (0.65,{\y-0.12}) .. ($(R)+(0,-0.12)$);
      }
      \filldraw[fill=white,draw=black,thick] (L) circle (4pt);
      \filldraw[fill=white,draw=black,thick] (R) circle (4pt);
      \node[align=center] at (0,-1.55)
        {\(V=2,\ E=3,\ F=3\)\\\(N_c^{V-E+F}=N_c^2\)};
    \end{scope}

    \begin{scope}[shift={(12.8,0)}]
      \node[font=\small] at (0,2.25) {one-handle theta graph};
      \coordinate (L) at (-1.45,0);
      \coordinate (R) at (1.45,0);
      \draw[blue!65!black,very thick]
        (L) .. controls (-0.65,0.9) and (0.65,0.9) .. (R);
      \draw[blue!65!black,very thick]
        ($(L)+(0,-0.12)$) .. controls (-0.65,0.78) and
        (0.65,0.78) .. ($(R)+(0,-0.12)$);
      \draw[purple!70!black,very thick,preaction={draw=white,line width=5pt}]
        (L) .. controls (-0.85,-0.55) and (0.85,0.55) .. (R);
      \draw[purple!70!black,very thick,preaction={draw=white,line width=5pt}]
        ($(L)+(0,-0.12)$) .. controls (-0.85,-0.67) and
        (0.85,0.43) .. ($(R)+(0,-0.12)$);
      \draw[red!70!black,very thick,preaction={draw=white,line width=5pt}]
        (L) .. controls (-0.85,0.55) and (0.85,-0.55) .. (R);
      \draw[red!70!black,very thick,preaction={draw=white,line width=5pt}]
        ($(L)+(0,-0.12)$) .. controls (-0.85,0.43) and
        (0.85,-0.67) .. ($(R)+(0,-0.12)$);
      \filldraw[fill=white,draw=black,thick] (L) circle (4pt);
      \filldraw[fill=white,draw=black,thick] (R) circle (4pt);
      \node[align=center] at (0,-1.55)
        {\(V=2,\ E=3,\ F=1\)\\\(N_c^{V-E+F}=N_c^0\)};
    \end{scope}
  \end{tikzpicture}
  }
  \caption{The adjoint propagator is represented by two oriented color lines.
  Thickening a double-line graph gives an oriented surface.  The planar and
  one-handle theta graphs have the same number of vertices and propagators;
  the nonplanar routing has two fewer closed color faces and is therefore
  suppressed by \(N_c^{-2}\) at fixed \(\lambda=g^2N_c\).}
  \label{fig:qcd-large-n-double-line-topology}
\end{figure}

The power counting is most transparent after the gauge-fixed adjoint
quadratic form and all adjoint interaction monomials are written in the
single-trace form
\begin{equation}
  S_{\rm adj}
  =
  \frac{N_c}{\lambda}
  \int\dd^4x\,
  \operatorname{tr}_{\square}
  \left(
    \frac12\Phi K\Phi+\sum_{\alpha} c_\alpha\,\Phi_{\alpha_1}\cdots
    \Phi_{\alpha_{r_\alpha}}
  \right),
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-single-trace-action-form}
\end{equation}
where \(\Phi\) denotes adjoint gauge, ghost, or auxiliary fields, \(K\) is the
appropriate kinetic operator including spacetime and Lorentz indices, and the
\(c_\alpha\) are \(N_c\)-independent tensors once \(\lambda\) is held fixed.
This rewriting is only a normalization of perturbative variables; it is not a
new definition of QCD.  It records the same fact already visible from
\[
  \frac1{4g^2}\operatorname{tr}_{\square}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}
  =
  \frac{N_c}{4\lambda}\operatorname{tr}_{\square}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}.
\]
With the normalization \eqref{eq:qcd-large-n-single-trace-action-form}, each
adjoint propagator contributes a factor \(\lambda/N_c\), each single-trace
adjoint vertex contributes a factor \(N_c/\lambda\), and each closed color
face contributes a factor \(N_c\) from summing an index from \(1\) to \(N_c\).
Spacetime loop integrals and Lorentz numerators multiply this color factor but
do not alter the topological exponent.

Let \(\Gamma\) be a connected adjoint ribbon graph with \(V\) single-trace
vertices, \(E\) adjoint propagators, and \(F\) closed color faces.  Thickening
the ribbons and vertices produces a compact oriented surface \(\Sigma_\Gamma\)
possibly with boundaries.  If the surface has genus \(h\) and \(b\) boundaries,
then
\begin{equation}
  \chi(\Sigma_\Gamma)
  =
  V-E+F
  =
  2-2h-b .
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-euler-characteristic}
\end{equation}
Therefore a fixed ribbon graph contributes
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal A_\Gamma
  =
  N_c^{V-E+F}\lambda^{E-V}\,
  \mathcal I_\Gamma(\lambda,\mu,\{p\})
  =
  N_c^{2-2h-b}\lambda^{E-V}\,
  \mathcal I_\Gamma(\lambda,\mu,\{p\}),
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-ribbon-power}
\end{equation}
up to the explicitly subleading terms generated by the
\(-N_c^{-1}\delta^i{}_j\delta^k{}_{\ell}\) part of
\eqref{eq:qcd-large-n-su-completeness}.  The factor
\(\mathcal I_\Gamma\) contains the regulated momentum integrals, tensor
contractions, signs, and scheme-dependent finite parts.  Equation
\eqref{eq:qcd-large-n-ribbon-power} is the topological content of the
't Hooft expansion: at fixed \(\lambda\), changing the genus by one suppresses
a connected contribution by \(N_c^{-2}\).

The theta graph in figure~\ref{fig:qcd-large-n-double-line-topology} gives the
elementary computation.  Both routings have \(V=2\) cubic vertices and
\(E=3\) propagators, hence both carry the same power \(\lambda^{E-V}=\lambda\).
The planar routing has \(F=3\), so
\[
  \mathcal A_{\rm planar}\sim N_c^{2}\lambda\,\mathcal I_{\rm planar}.
\]
The crossed routing thickens to a torus with \(F=1\), so
\[
  \mathcal A_{\rm one\ handle}\sim N_c^{0}\lambda\,
  \mathcal I_{\rm one\ handle}.
\]
For comparable regulated integrals at the same external data, the color
topology alone gives
\begin{equation}
  \frac{\mathcal A_{\rm one\ handle}}{\mathcal A_{\rm planar}}
  =
  O(N_c^{-2})
  \qquad (\lambda \text{ fixed}).
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-nonplanar-suppression}
\end{equation}
This is a finite color-index statement at each fixed ribbon graph.  It does
not by itself prove convergence of the perturbation series, nor does it define
the four-dimensional continuum gauge theory nonperturbatively.

\begin{controlledapproximation}[Genus truncation as a regulated observable
statement]
\label{ca:qcd-large-n-genus-truncation-remainder-bound}
Let \(X_{N_c,\Lambda,L}(\lambda;\mathcal J)\) be a regulated color-singlet
observable: the ultraviolet regulator, spatial volume, boundary conditions,
operator/source normalization, and coupling coordinate have all been fixed.
After the overall \(N_c\)-power predicted by
\eqref{eq:qcd-large-n-ribbon-power} has been factored out, a controlled
large-\(N_c\) genus expansion through genus \(H\) is the statement that on a
declared coupling and source window
\begin{equation}
  X_{N_c,\Lambda,L}
  =
  \sum_{h=0}^{H}N_c^{-2h}X_{h,\Lambda,L}
  +
  R_{H,N_c,\Lambda,L},
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-genus-truncated-observable}
\end{equation}
with bounds
\begin{equation}
  |X_{h,\Lambda,L}|\le M_h,
  \qquad
  |R_{H,N_c,\Lambda,L}|
  \le
  \varepsilon_H N_c^{-2H-2}.
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-genus-truncation-bound}
\end{equation}
The ribbon-graph Euler characteristic supplies the allowed powers of
\(1/N_c\).  The coefficient bounds and the remainder bound are additional
analytic information about the regulated QCD observable.  Without them,
``planar dominance'' is a formal color-topology classification, not a
controlled approximation to a physical matrix element or correlation
function.

The regulator labels in
\eqref{eq:qcd-large-n-genus-truncated-observable} are load-bearing.  A
continuum, infinite-volume, or massless limit may be taken after the
large-\(N_c\) limit only if the bounds are uniform in the corresponding
regulator variables, or if a separate argument proves equality of the
interchanged limits:
\[
  \lim_{\Lambda,L}\lim_{N_c\to\infty}X_{N_c,\Lambda,L}
  =
  \lim_{N_c\to\infty}\lim_{\Lambda,L}X_{N_c,\Lambda,L}.
\]
Large-\(N_c\) factorization, volume reduction, baryon scaling, and QCD-string
interpretations therefore require more than the fixed-graph power count: they
require the regulated state, observable class, spectral or line-operator
data, and the uniformity needed for the limit being asserted.  Contributions
which are smaller than every power of \(1/N_c\), or phase choices which become
visible only after an infinite-volume limit, are invisible to the genus
polynomial at any fixed \(H\) but can still affect a nonperturbative
large-\(N_c\) statement.
\end{controlledapproximation}

Single-trace operator insertions are counted as boundaries.  For adjoint words
\[
  \mathcal O_r(x)
  =
  \operatorname{tr}_{\square}
  \bigl(\Phi_{r_1}(x)\cdots \Phi_{r_{\ell_r}}(x)\bigr),
\]
the connected genus-\(h\) contribution to an \(m\)-point function of
unnormalized single traces scales as
\begin{equation}
  \bigl\langle
  \mathcal O_1(x_1)\cdots \mathcal O_m(x_m)
  \bigr\rangle_{\!c,h}
  =
  O\!\left(N_c^{2-2h-m}\right),
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-single-trace-scaling}
\end{equation}
again at fixed \(\lambda\) and fixed operator words.  If
\(\widehat{\mathcal O}_r=N_c^{-1}\mathcal O_r\), then
\[
  \bigl\langle
  \widehat{\mathcal O}_1\cdots \widehat{\mathcal O}_m
  \bigr\rangle_{\!c,h}
  =
  O\!\left(N_c^{2-2h-2m}\right).
\]
In particular,
\begin{equation}
  \bigl\langle
  \widehat{\mathcal O}_1\widehat{\mathcal O}_2
  \bigr\rangle_c
  =
  O(N_c^{-2}),
  \qquad
  \langle \widehat{\mathcal O}_1\widehat{\mathcal O}_2\rangle
  =
  \langle \widehat{\mathcal O}_1\rangle
  \langle \widehat{\mathcal O}_2\rangle+O(N_c^{-2}).
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-factorization}
\end{equation}
This large-\(N_c\) factorization is often the most useful nonperturbative
consequence of the counting: it says that fluctuations of normalized
single-trace observables are suppressed in the planar limit, provided the
large-\(N_c\) limit of the regulated theory exists.

\subsection{Eguchi--Kawai Volume Reduction}
\label{subsec:qcd-eguchi-kawai-volume-reduction}

Large-\(N_c\) factorization suggests a further possibility: in some
center-symmetric large-\(N_c\) regimes, single-trace Wilson-loop expectation
values on a large lattice may be obtained from a reduced matrix model with
fewer sites.  Double-line counting supplies factorization of normalized
single traces; volume reduction additionally requires matching the
finite-regulator loop equations in a center-symmetric sector.

Let \(\Lambda_L=(\mathbb Z/L\mathbb Z)^d\) be a periodic hypercubic lattice,
and let \(U_\mu(x)\in SU(N_c)\) be the link from \(x\) to
\(x+\hat\mu\).  The pure-gauge Wilson measure is
\[
  \dd\nu_L(U)
  =
  Z_L^{-1}
  \exp\left\{
    bN_c\sum_{x\in\Lambda_L}\sum_{\mu<\nu}
    \operatorname{Re}\operatorname{Tr}_{\square}
    U_{\mu\nu}(x)
  \right\}
  \prod_{x,\mu}\dd U_\mu(x),
\]
where
\[
  U_{\mu\nu}(x)=
  U_\mu(x)U_\nu(x+\hat\mu)
  U_\mu(x+\hat\nu)^{-1}U_\nu(x)^{-1}.
\]
The one-site Eguchi--Kawai reduced model has \(d\) matrices
\(V_\mu\in SU(N_c)\) and measure
\begin{equation}
  \dd\nu_{\rm EK}(V)
  =
  Z_{\rm EK}^{-1}
  \exp\left\{
    bN_c\sum_{\mu<\nu}
    \operatorname{Re}\operatorname{Tr}_{\square}
    V_\mu V_\nu V_\mu^{-1}V_\nu^{-1}
  \right\}
  \prod_\mu \dd V_\mu .
  \label{eq:qcd-eguchi-kawai-reduced-measure}
\end{equation}
The parameter \(b\) is a lattice coupling coordinate.  The present discussion
uses it at fixed regulator; continuum statements require a separate scaling
limit.

A path word in the reduced model is a sequence
\[
  p=((\mu_1,\epsilon_1),\ldots,(\mu_\ell,\epsilon_\ell)),
  \qquad \epsilon_r=\pm1 ,
\]
with associated matrix
\[
  V(p)=V_{\mu_1}^{\epsilon_1}\cdots V_{\mu_\ell}^{\epsilon_\ell}
\]
and displacement vector
\begin{equation}
  n_\rho(p)=
  \sum_{r=1}^{\ell}\epsilon_r\,\delta_{\rho\mu_r}.
  \label{eq:qcd-ek-word-displacement}
\end{equation}
The normalized trace
\[
  W_{\rm red}(p)=\frac1{N_c}\operatorname{Tr}_{\square}V(p)
\]
is gauge invariant under simultaneous conjugation
\(V_\mu\mapsto \Omega V_\mu\Omega^{-1}\) even when \(n(p)\ne0\).  This is
precisely why the reduced model needs an additional center-selection rule:
in the extended lattice theory, an open path is not a Wilson-loop observable,
whereas its reduced trace would otherwise be an allowed matrix observable.

The reduced action is invariant under the independent center transformations
\begin{equation}
  V_\mu\longmapsto z_\mu V_\mu,\qquad
  z_\mu\in Z(SU(N_c))\simeq\mathbb Z_{N_c}.
  \label{eq:qcd-ek-center-transformation}
\end{equation}
Under this transformation,
\begin{equation}
  W_{\rm red}(p)
  \longmapsto
  \left(\prod_{\rho=1}^{d}z_\rho^{\,n_\rho(p)}\right)
  W_{\rm red}(p).
  \label{eq:qcd-ek-open-word-center-charge}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Center selection for reduced open words.}
Assume the reduced finite-\(N_c\) state is invariant under
\eqref{eq:qcd-ek-center-transformation}.  If the center charge of a word is
nontrivial, namely if \(n_\rho(p)\not\equiv0\pmod{N_c}\) for at least one
\(\rho\), then
\[
  \bigl\langle W_{\rm red}(p)\bigr\rangle_{\rm EK}=0 .
\]
Consequently, if a charged reduced word has a nonzero expectation value in a
chosen large-\(N_c\) state, that state cannot implement ordinary
Eguchi--Kawai volume reduction for Wilson loops.
Choose a direction \(\rho\) for which
\(n_\rho(p)\not\equiv0\pmod{N_c}\).  There exists
\(z_\rho\in\mathbb Z_{N_c}\) such that \(z_\rho^{n_\rho(p)}\ne1\).  Invariance
of the state gives
\[
  \langle W_{\rm red}(p)\rangle_{\rm EK}
  =
  z_\rho^{n_\rho(p)}
  \langle W_{\rm red}(p)\rangle_{\rm EK},
\]
where all other \(z_\sigma\)'s are set to \(1\).  The prefactor differs from
one, hence the expectation value is zero.  If a charged expectation is
nonzero, the assumed center invariance is false.  Such an expectation is an
extra reduced-model observable with no counterpart as a closed Wilson loop in the
unreduced lattice theory, so the reduced loop equations cannot be identified
with the unreduced Wilson-loop equations without further modification.

The loop-equation form of volume reduction can now be stated cleanly.

\begin{definition}[Volume-reduction structure for Wilson loops]
\label{def:qcd-volume-reduction-datum}
A volume-reduction structure for pure large-\(N_c\) lattice Yang--Mills Wilson
loops consists of the following assumptions and regulator objects:
\begin{enumerate}
\item a sequence of finite-volume Wilson lattice measures with fixed lattice
coupling coordinate \(b\), and a sequence of reduced measures such as
\eqref{eq:qcd-eguchi-kawai-reduced-measure} or a twisted/quenched variant;
\item a class \(\mathcal C\) of closed lattice paths whose normalized
Wilson-loop expectations have large-\(N_c\) limits;
\item translation invariance of the large-volume loop expectations and
large-\(N_c\) factorization of normalized single-trace loop observables;
\item unbroken reduced center symmetry strong enough to enforce the vanishing
of all reduced open words that appear in the loop equations for
\(\mathcal C\);
\item uniqueness, in the selected center-symmetric sector, of the solution to
the limiting Schwinger--Dyson loop equations with the specified boundary and
reflection-positivity data.
\end{enumerate}
When these assumptions and regulator objects are supplied, volume reduction means
\begin{equation}
  \lim_{N_c\to\infty}\lim_{L\to\infty}
  \left\langle\frac1{N_c}\operatorname{Tr}U(C)\right\rangle_L
  =
  \lim_{N_c\to\infty}
  \left\langle\frac1{N_c}\operatorname{Tr}V(C)\right\rangle_{\rm red}
  \label{eq:qcd-volume-reduction-loop-equality}
\end{equation}
for every \(C\in\mathcal C\), where \(V(C)\) is obtained from the sequence of
directions in the lattice path \(C\).  The equality concerns single-trace
Wilson-loop coordinates at fixed regulator.  Colored local fields, baryons,
finite-\(N_c\) spectra, and continuum limits require additional definitions
and additional limiting statements.
\end{definition}

The content of this structure is not decorative.  In a Schwinger--Dyson equation
for a Wilson loop, varying a link on the loop produces terms in which the loop
splits at repeated visits to the varied link.  In the unreduced theory these
split loops may be based at different lattice sites; translation invariance
and large-\(N_c\) factorization replace their joint expectation by the product
of one-loop expectations.  In the reduced model the corresponding matrix words
are all based at the single site, and open words can appear algebraically.
The center-selection rule above is what removes the charged open words.  The
uniqueness clause in
Definition~\ref{def:qcd-volume-reduction-datum} is also load-bearing: two
different center-symmetric solutions of the same limiting loop equations would
not be distinguished by the formal loop-equation matching alone.

\begin{controlledapproximation}[Status of Eguchi--Kawai reduction]
\label{ca:qcd-eguchi-kawai-status}
The original one-site Eguchi--Kawai model is a valid reduced candidate only in
regimes where its center symmetry is not spontaneously broken in the
large-\(N_c\) limit.  At weak lattice coupling in dimensions larger than two,
the center of the original one-site model breaks; the naive model then
produces a reduced large-\(N_c\) state with charged reduced-word expectation
values rather than the desired large-volume Wilson-loop theory.
Quenched Eguchi--Kawai, twisted Eguchi--Kawai, partial reduction, and adjoint
matter with periodic boundary conditions are different attempts to stabilize
the needed center realization.  Each changes the finite-regulator construction and
therefore requires its own loop-equation and symmetry analysis.  This
monograph uses volume reduction only when the relevant center, factorization,
and uniqueness hypotheses are explicitly part of the statement.
\end{controlledapproximation}

Fundamental quarks introduce boundaries rather than handles.  A closed quark
loop carries one fundamental color sum and one flavor sum.  In the fixed-flavor
't Hooft limit \(N_f\) is held fixed, and a graph with \(b_q\) quark-loop
boundaries scales as
\begin{equation}
  N_f^{b_q}N_c^{2-2h-b_q}.
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-quark-boundary-fixed-nf}
\end{equation}
Thus each additional closed quark loop is suppressed by one power of
\(N_c^{-1}\) relative to a gluonic planar vacuum contribution.  In the
Veneziano limit, by contrast, \(N_f/N_c\) is held fixed; the factor
\[
  N_f^{b_q}N_c^{2-2h-b_q}
  =
  \left(\frac{N_f}{N_c}\right)^{b_q}N_c^{2-2h}
\]
shows that quark-loop boundaries are no longer suppressed.  The Banks--Zaks
discussion above naturally belongs to this Veneziano scaling when
\(N_f/N_c\) is tuned close to \(11/2\).

The same counting explains the leading \(N_c\)-dependence of hadronic
interactions in a confining large-\(N_c\) theory, subject to the separate
spectral assumptions stated below.  A mesonic color-singlet operator
\(M_{IJ}=\bar q_I q_J\) has a connected \(m\)-point function of order \(N_c\)
when all insertions lie on one quark boundary.  The canonically normalized
meson source is therefore \(N_c^{-1/2}M_{IJ}\), and connected \(m\)-meson
amplitudes scale as \(N_c^{1-m/2}\).  Glueball operators normalized to have
order-one connected two-point functions have connected \(m\)-point couplings
of order \(N_c^{2-m}\).

\subsection{\texorpdfstring{Large-\(N_c\) Baryons and Spin-Flavor Coordinates}{Large-Nc Baryons and Spin-Flavor Coordinates}}
\label{subsec:qcd-large-n-baryon-spin-flavor}

Baryons require a separate order of limits and a separate normalization.  In
an \(SU(N_c)\) theory with \(N_f\) fundamental quark flavors, a local baryon
operator in a chosen spin-flavor channel has the color-antisymmetric local
form
\begin{equation}
  B_{\mathcal A}(x)
  =
  \epsilon_{i_1\cdots i_{N_c}}\,
  \mathcal A^{I_1\alpha_1,\ldots,I_{N_c}\alpha_{N_c}}\,
  q^{i_1}_{I_1\alpha_1}(x)\cdots
  q^{i_{N_c}}_{I_{N_c}\alpha_{N_c}}(x),
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-baryon-operator}
\end{equation}
where \(i_r\) are color indices, \(I_r\) are flavor indices,
\(\alpha_r\) are spinor indices, and \(\mathcal A\) is a tensor selecting the
spin-flavor channel.  The color tensor is totally antisymmetric.  Therefore,
for baryons built from quarks in the same spatial orbital, the remaining
spin-flavor wavefunction is symmetric under exchange of the \(N_c\) quarks.
This is a kinematic consequence of Fermi statistics and the
\(\epsilon\)-contraction, not a dynamical theorem about the spectrum.

Take the large-\(N_c\) limit with \(N_f\) fixed, quark masses fixed in
\(N_c\)-independent units, and the spatial volume sent to infinity in the
usual order needed to define particle states.  If a baryon state created by
\eqref{eq:qcd-large-n-baryon-operator} exists and has a Hartree description in
which each quark moves in an \(N_c\)-independent mean field, then its leading
mass is \(O(N_c)\).  Indeed there are \(N_c\) one-body contributions, while
the two-body estimate gives
\begin{equation}
  \binom{N_c}{2}\,g^2
  =
  \frac{N_c(N_c-1)}2\,\frac{\lambda}{N_c}
  =
  \frac{\lambda}{2}(N_c-1),
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-baryon-pair-counting}
\end{equation}
which is also \(O(N_c)\) at fixed \(\lambda\).  The Hartree statement is a
controlled large-\(N_c\) organization only after the existence of such
color-singlet baryon states has been assumed or established in a regulator.
It is not a derivation of baryons from the continuum Yang--Mills path integral.

Spin and flavor splittings are subleading in the same limit.  If the baryon is
described at low energy by a slowly rotating spin-flavor collective coordinate
with moment of inertia \(I_{\rm rot}=O(N_c)\), then the rotor contribution has
the form
\begin{equation}
  \Delta M_J
  =
  \frac{J(J+1)}{2I_{\rm rot}},
  \label{eq:qcd-large-n-baryon-rotor-splitting}
\end{equation}
so fixed-spin splittings are \(O(N_c^{-1})\).  In theories with chiral
symmetry breaking, the same scaling is compatible with a soliton description
in the mesonic effective theory: \(F_\pi^2=O(N_c)\), meson interaction
vertices have the large-\(N_c\) scaling \(g_m=O(N_c^{1-m/2})\), and a soliton
mass built from the mesonic action is \(O(N_c)\).  The spin-flavor multiplet
structure often
called contracted \(SU(2N_f)\) is the algebraic form of the large-\(N_c\)
consistency conditions on baryon matrix elements, for example on pion-baryon
scattering.  In this monograph it is used only with the displayed hypotheses:
fixed \(N_f\), a confining theory with baryon states, and a low-energy regime
where the collective-coordinate description is valid.  In the Veneziano limit
with \(N_f/N_c\) fixed, the spin-flavor representation theory and baryon
entropy grow differently and must be analyzed as a distinct limit.

For two light flavors the contracted spin-flavor algebra can be written
without appealing to a quark-model Hamiltonian.  It is the large-\(N_c\)
limit of the exact one-body spin-flavor algebra acting on the symmetric
spin-flavor part of a color-singlet baryon source.

\begin{definition}[Two-flavor spin-flavor one-body generators]
\label{def:qcd-two-flavor-spin-flavor-generators}
On the spin-flavor space
\[
  \left(\mathbb C^2_{\rm spin}\otimes\mathbb C^2_{\rm isospin}\right)^{\otimes N_c}
\]
write \(\sigma_r^i\) and \(\tau_r^a\) for Pauli matrices acting on the spin
and isospin factors of the \(r\)-th quark slot.  Define
\begin{equation}
  J^i=\sum_{r=1}^{N_c}\frac{\sigma_r^i}{2},
  \qquad
  I^a=\sum_{r=1}^{N_c}\frac{\tau_r^a}{2},
  \qquad
  G^{ia}=\sum_{r=1}^{N_c}\frac{\sigma_r^i\tau_r^a}{4}.
  \label{eq:qcd-two-flavor-spin-flavor-generators}
\end{equation}
Here \(J^i\) generates spatial spin, \(I^a\) generates isospin, and
\(G^{ia}\) is the mixed spin-isospin one-body generator.  The normalization is
fixed by the displayed Pauli matrices; it is independent of the gauge-group
trace convention used in the Yang--Mills action.
\end{definition}

\paragraph{Contraction of the two-flavor baryon spin-flavor algebra.}
The generators of Definition~\ref{def:qcd-two-flavor-spin-flavor-generators}
satisfy the exact finite-\(N_c\) commutation relations
\begin{align}
  [J^i,J^j]&=\ii\varepsilon^{ijk}J^k,
  &
  [I^a,I^b]&=\ii\varepsilon^{abc}I^c,
  &
  [J^i,I^a]&=0,
  \label{eq:qcd-spin-flavor-ji-algebra}\\
  [J^i,G^{ja}]&=\ii\varepsilon^{ijk}G^{ka},
  &
  [I^a,G^{ib}]&=\ii\varepsilon^{abc}G^{ic},
  \label{eq:qcd-spin-flavor-g-vector}\\
  [G^{ia},G^{jb}]
  &=
  \frac{\ii}{4}\delta^{ij}\varepsilon^{abc}I^c
  +
  \frac{\ii}{4}\delta^{ab}\varepsilon^{ijk}J^k .
  \label{eq:qcd-spin-flavor-g-commutator}
\end{align}
Set \(X^{ia}=G^{ia}/N_c\).  On any sequence of baryon states whose matrix
elements of \(J^i\) and \(I^a\) grow no faster than \(O(N_c)\), the
commutator of the rescaled mixed generators obeys
\begin{equation}
  [X^{ia},X^{jb}]
  =
  \frac{\ii}{4N_c^2}\delta^{ij}\varepsilon^{abc}I^c
  +
  \frac{\ii}{4N_c^2}\delta^{ab}\varepsilon^{ijk}J^k
  =
  O(N_c^{-1})
  \label{eq:qcd-contracted-su4-xx}
\end{equation}
