subtraction supplies the terms with \(P(x)\).  The apparent poles are
removable because \(P(y)-P(x)\) is divisible by \(y-x\).

Let \(P(x)=\sum_{m=0}^N p_mx^m\), and put
\(H_m=\sum_{r=1}^m r^{-1}\), \(H_0=0\).  Evaluating the beta integrals in
\eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-finite-polynomial-action} gives the explicit coefficient
operator
\begin{align}
  \mathcal E[P](x)
  &:=
  \frac1{C_F}\int_0^1\dd y\,V(x,y)\varphi_P(y)
  \notag\\
  &=
  6(1-x)
  \left[
    \sum_{m=0}^N p_m\frac{x^{m+2}}{m+2}
    -\frac{x^2}{2}P(x)
    -\sum_{m=1}^N p_m x^{m+1}(H_{m+1}-1)
  \right]
  \notag\\
  &\quad
  +6x
  \left[
    \sum_{m=0}^N p_m
    \left(
      \frac1{(m+1)(m+2)}
      -\frac{x^{m+1}}{m+1}
      +\frac{x^{m+2}}{m+2}
    \right)
    -\frac{(1-x)^2}{2}P(x)
  \right.
  \notag\\
  &\hspace{7.2em}\left.
    +\sum_{m=1}^N p_m\sum_{j=0}^{m-1}x^j
    \left(
      \frac1{(m-j)(m-j+1)}
      -\frac{x^{m-j}}{m-j}
      +\frac{x^{m-j+1}}{m-j+1}
    \right)
  \right].
  \label{eq:qcd-erbl-coefficient-operator}
\end{align}
For \(P=P_n\), the coefficients are fixed by
\[
  P_0(x)=1,\qquad P_1(x)=3(2x-1),
\]
and by the Gegenbauer recurrence
\begin{equation}
  (n+1)P_{n+1}(x)
  =
  (2n+3)(2x-1)P_n(x)-(n+2)P_{n-1}(x).
  \label{eq:qcd-erbl-gegenbauer-recurrence}
\end{equation}
Substitution of this recurrence into the coefficient operator
\eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-coefficient-operator} gives, by induction on \(n\),
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal E[P_n](x)
  =
  -\frac12
  \left[
    1-\frac{2}{(n+1)(n+2)}
    +4\sum_{k=2}^{n+1}\frac1k
  \right]
  6x(1-x)P_n(x).
  \label{eq:qcd-erbl-polynomial-identity}
\end{equation}
The induction is finite algebra in the polynomial ring \(\mathbb Q[x]\):
\eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-coefficient-operator} gives the action on each monomial,
\eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-gegenbauer-recurrence} gives the next \(P_n\), and the
new harmonic term changes by \(H_{n+2}-H_{n+1}=1/(n+2)\).  The cases
\(n=0,1\) start the induction; \(n=0\) is the axial-current normalization
already expressed by \(\int_0^1\dd x\,V(x,y)=0\).  Restoring the overall
factor \(C_F\) proves \eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-eigenvalue-equation}.  The factor
\(1/2\) in \eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-eigenvalue-equation} is tied to the convention
that the evolution equation was written with \(\alpha_s/(2\pi)\) in
\eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-evolution}; in the common convention in which the moment
equation is written with \(\alpha_s/(4\pi)\), the same
\(\gamma_n^{(0)}\) appears without this extra displayed factor.
\end{proof}

The diagonal basis has a representation-theoretic origin: the collinear
conformal group acting on two light-ray quark fields has
\(SL(2,\mathbb R)\) quadratic Casimir diagonalized by
\(x(1-x)C_n^{3/2}(2x-1)\), and the one-loop kernel
\eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-kernel} is compatible with this action after the plus
subtraction.  The proof above keeps the normalization independent of that
interpretation by reducing the statement to the finite polynomial identity
\eqref{eq:qcd-erbl-polynomial-identity}.

Therefore a pion LCDA may be expanded as
\begin{equation}
  \varphi_\pi(x,\mu)
  =
  6x(1-x)
  \left[
  1+\sum_{n=1}^{\infty}a_n(\mu)C_n^{3/2}(2x-1)
  \right],
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-lcda-gegenbauer-expansion}
\end{equation}
with odd \(a_n\) vanishing in the isospin-symmetric charge-conjugation
channel.  At leading order
\begin{equation}
  \mu\frac{\dd}{\dd\mu}a_n(\mu)
  =
  -\frac{\alpha_s(\mu)}{4\pi}\gamma_n^{(0)}a_n(\mu)
  +O(\alpha_s^2),
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-gegenbauer-moment-rg}
\end{equation}
and asymptotic freedom drives all \(n\ge1\) moments to zero.  The ultraviolet
fixed shape is consequently
\begin{equation}
  \varphi_\pi^{\rm as}(x)=6x(1-x).
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-asymptotic-lcda}
\end{equation}
The approach to this shape is logarithmic and scheme dependent at finite
scale.  The statement \eqref{eq:qcd-pion-asymptotic-lcda} is therefore an
asymptotic consequence of the light-ray anomalous dimensions, not an empirical
model for the pion at moderate momentum transfer.

\subsection{Hard Exclusive Form Factors and Boundary Regimes}
\label{subsec:qcd-hard-exclusive-form-factors}

The spacelike charged-pion electromagnetic form factor is defined by
\begin{equation}
  \bra{\pi^+(P')}J^\mu_{\rm em}(0)\ket{\pi^+(P)}
  =
  (P+P')^\mu F_\pi(Q^2),
  \qquad
  Q^2:=-(P'-P)^2>0 .
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-em-form-factor-definition}
\end{equation}
At leading twist and leading order in the hard coefficient,
\begin{equation}
  F_\pi(Q^2)
  =
  \frac{4\pi\alpha_s(\mu)C_F}{N_c}\,
  \frac{f_\pi^2}{Q^2}
  \left[
  \int_0^1\dd x\,\frac{\varphi_\pi(x,\mu)}{x}
  \right]
  \left[
  \int_0^1\dd y\,\frac{\varphi_\pi(y,\mu)}{y}
  \right]
  +O(\alpha_s^2,Q^{-4})_{\rm fac}.
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-form-factor-factorization}
\end{equation}
The subscript ``fac'' means that the power remainder belongs to the
factorization scheme of Definition~\ref{def:qcd-exclusive-pion-factorization-scheme};
endpoint sensitivity can modify the practical convergence at accessible
\(Q^2\) unless controlled by an additional Sudakov or nonperturbative input.

\paragraph{Derivation of the leading hard coefficient.}
Project each pion onto the color-singlet leading-twist collinear pair defined
by \eqref{eq:qcd-pion-lcda-definition}.  A single hard gluon must transfer
large transverse momentum between the two collinear quark lines; without this
exchange the electromagnetic current cannot convert the initial collinear
color-singlet into the final one at fixed large \(Q^2\).  The tree hard
subgraph contains one gluon propagator of virtuality \(O(Q^2)\) and one
internal quark propagator of virtuality \(O(Q^2)\).  Contracting the spin
projectors fixed by the axial matrix element gives the numerator
proportional to \(P\cdot P'\), and the color-singlet projection gives
\[
  \frac1{N_c}\,t^a_{ij}t^a_{ji}
  =
  \frac{C_F}{N_c}.
\]
The two endpoint denominators are the light-cone momentum fractions carried by
the active quarks.  With the LCDA normalization
\eqref{eq:qcd-pion-lcda-normalization}, the sum of the two photon attachments
to the charged pion gives
\eqref{eq:qcd-pion-form-factor-factorization}.  No on-shell colored external
state appears in the final formula; colored partons were only projectors used
to compute the short-distance coefficient in the chosen factorization scheme.

Substituting \(\varphi_\pi^{\rm as}\) gives
\begin{equation}
  Q^2F_\pi(Q^2)
  \sim
  \frac{36\pi\alpha_s(Q)C_F}{N_c}\,f_\pi^2 .
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-form-factor-asymptotic-general}
\end{equation}
For \(N_c=3\), this is
\[
  Q^2F_\pi(Q^2)\sim 16\pi\alpha_{\rm ht}(Q) f_\pi^2
\]
in the common half-trace convention.  Equivalently, if one uses the
phenomenological convention \(f_\pi^{(130)}=\sqrt2\,f_\pi\), the same statement
is often written as \(Q^2F_\pi\sim
8\pi\alpha_{\rm ht}(f_\pi^{(130)})^2\).  The monograph keeps the
Chapter~\ref{ch:volume-ii-21} convention \(f_\pi\) fixed, so
\eqref{eq:qcd-pion-form-factor-asymptotic-general} is the convention-safe
formula.

The neutral-pion transition form factor is defined by
\begin{equation}
  \ii\int\dd^4x\,\ee^{\ii q\cdot x}
  \bra{\pi^0(P)}
  \operatorname T\,J^\mu_{\rm em}(x)J^\nu_{\rm em}(0)
  \ket{\vac}
  =
  \epsilon^{\mu\nu\rho\sigma}q_\rho k_\sigma\,
  F_{\pi^0\gamma\gamma^*}(Q^2),
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-transition-form-factor-definition}
\end{equation}
with \(k^2=0\), \(q^2=-Q^2\), and \(P=q+k\).  The low-energy endpoint is fixed
by the anomaly calculation of Chapters~\ref{ch:volume-ii-20} and
\ref{ch:volume-ii-21}:
\begin{equation}
  F_{\pi^0\gamma\gamma^*}(0)=\frac1{4\pi^2f_\pi}
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-transition-anomaly-normalization}
\end{equation}
in the present \(f_\pi\) convention.  At large spacelike \(Q^2\), leading
twist factorization gives
\begin{equation}
  F_{\pi^0\gamma\gamma^*}(Q^2)
  =
  \frac{f_\pi}{3Q^2}
  \int_0^1\dd x\,\varphi_\pi(x,\mu)
  \left(\frac1x+\frac1{1-x}\right)
  +O(\alpha_s,Q^{-4})_{\rm fac}.
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-transition-factorization}
\end{equation}
For the asymptotic LCDA,
\begin{equation}
  Q^2F_{\pi^0\gamma\gamma^*}(Q^2)\longrightarrow 2f_\pi .
  \label{eq:qcd-pion-transition-asymptotic}
\end{equation}
In the \(f_\pi^{(130)}=\sqrt2 f_\pi\) convention this is
\(\sqrt2 f_\pi^{(130)}\).  The anomaly value
\eqref{eq:qcd-pion-transition-anomaly-normalization} and the asymptotic value
\eqref{eq:qcd-pion-transition-asymptotic} constrain two different boundary
regimes of the same form factor.  They do not imply a unique interpolation.
Published high-\(Q^2\) measurements are therefore tests of the approach to the
factorized regime and of LCDA shape assumptions, not independent definitions
of the anomaly or of asymptotic freedom.

The BaBar/Belle tension in the neutral-pion transition form factor is best
formulated in exactly these terms.  A reported finite-\(Q^2\) trend can
challenge a chosen LCDA model, a treatment of endpoint regions, or an estimate
of power corrections.  It cannot by itself refute
\eqref{eq:qcd-pion-transition-anomaly-normalization} or
\eqref{eq:qcd-pion-transition-asymptotic}, because those two equations live at
opposite ends of the form factor and rest on different controlled arguments:
the first on the axial anomaly and the second on leading-twist factorization
plus ERBL evolution.  Any quantitative comparison to data must therefore state
the factorization scale, perturbative order, LCDA input, and power-correction
model before it is a QCD statement.

\begin{controlledapproximation}[Brodsky--Farrar scaling]
\label{ca:qcd-brodsky-farrar-scaling-status}
Consider a fixed-angle exclusive process
\[
  H_1+H_2\longrightarrow H_3+H_4
\]
whose leading Fock components contain \(n_i\) collinear elementary fields in
the corresponding leading-twist light-ray operator, and set
\(n=\sum_i n_i\).  If a leading-power exclusive factorization theorem holds,
if endpoint configurations are power suppressed in the specified scheme, and
if the hard coefficient is evaluated at momentum transfer of order
\(\sqrt s\), then
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal M(s,t)\sim s^{\,2-n/2}
  \quad\text{at fixed }t/s,
  \qquad
  \frac{\dd\sigma}{\dd t}\sim s^{\,2-n}.
  \label{eq:qcd-brodsky-farrar-counting}
\end{equation}
The derivation is the power counting of the hard coefficient after all
external hadrons have been replaced by their normalized leading-twist
distribution amplitudes.  It is not a dimensional-analysis argument applied to
hadron fields.  For example, a pion electromagnetic form factor has
\(n=4\) across the two pion light-ray operators and therefore scales as
\(F_\pi(Q^2)\sim Q^{-2}\), while fixed-angle
\(\pi\pi\to\pi\pi\) has \(n=8\) and gives
\(\dd\sigma/\dd t\sim s^{-6}\), up to logarithmic evolution and controlled
power corrections.
\end{controlledapproximation}

The pion \(S\)-matrix is thus accessed by different controlled descriptions in
different regions.  Chapter~\ref{ch:volume-ii-21} derives the chiral
low-energy amplitudes and anomaly couplings; Chapter~\ref{ch:volume-ii-07}
develops the analytic and dispersive language needed for the resonance
region; the present section gives the asymptotic exclusive regime governed by
light-ray factorization and asymptotic freedom.  These are not three theories.
They are three coordinate systems on matrix elements of the same QCD
operators, with different hypotheses controlling their errors.  A complete
nonperturbative construction of QCD would define the relevant matrix elements
first; chiral perturbation theory, dispersive methods, lattice calculations,
and hard exclusive factorization are then approximation or reconstruction
methods applied to that common object.

\section{Local Twist-Two Operators and Integer Moments}

The light-ray definition packages an infinite family of local operators.  For
quarks, the symmetric traceless spin-\(N\) operators have the form
\[
  \mathcal O_{q}^{\mu_1\cdots\mu_N}
  =
  \bar q\,
  \gamma^{(\mu_1}
  \ii D^{\mu_2}\cdots \ii D^{\mu_N)}
  q
  -
  \text{traces},
  \qquad N\ge1,
\]
with gauge-covariant derivatives in the appropriate representation.  The
parentheses denote symmetrization over Lorentz indices.  The gluon operators
are
\[
  \mathcal O_{g}^{\mu_1\cdots\mu_N}
  =
  F^{(\mu_1}{}_{\rho}\,
  \ii D^{\mu_2}\cdots\ii D^{\mu_{N-1}}
  F^{\mu_N)\rho}
  -
  \text{traces},
  \qquad N\ge2 .
\]
Both have twist two after subtracting traces.  Their hadron matrix elements
are fixed by Lorentz covariance up to scalar reduced matrix elements:
\[
  \bra{P}\mathcal O_{a}^{\mu_1\cdots\mu_N}(\mu)\ket{P}
  =
  2\,a_{a,N}(\mu)
  \bigl(P^{\mu_1}\cdots P^{\mu_N}-\text{traces}\bigr),
  \qquad a=q,g .
\]
The normalization of \(a_{a,N}\) is fixed already at tree level by replacing
the hadron with a single parton state.  Let
\(\ket{p,i,s}\) be a massless quark of flavor \(i\) and spin \(s\), and use
\(\bar u_s(p)\gamma^\mu u_s(p)=2p^\mu\).  Since each covariant derivative in
\(\mathcal O_{q_j}\) acts on the external plane wave as multiplication by
\(p^\mu\) at tree level,
\[
  \bra{p,i,s}\mathcal O_{q_j}^{\mu_1\cdots\mu_N}\ket{p,i,s}_{\rm tree}
  =
  2\delta_{ij}
  \bigl(p^{\mu_1}\cdots p^{\mu_N}-\text{traces}\bigr).
\]
Thus a quark target has
\[
  a_{q_j,N}^{\,q_i,{\rm tree}}=\delta_{ij},
  \qquad
  a_{g,N}^{\,q_i,{\rm tree}}=0 .
\]
For a single gluon state \(\ket{p,A,\lambda}\), the linearized field strength
is
\[
  F^{\mu\nu,A}(x)
  =
  \ii\bigl(p^\mu\varepsilon^\nu_\lambda
  -p^\nu\varepsilon^\mu_\lambda\bigr)\ee^{-\ii p\cdot x},
  \qquad
  p\cdot\varepsilon_\lambda=0 .
\]
The color- and spin-averaged partonic matrix element is
\[
  \frac{1}{2(N_c^2-1)}
  \sum_{A,\lambda}
  \bra{p,A,\lambda}\mathcal O_{g}^{\mu_1\cdots\mu_N}
  \ket{p,A,\lambda}_{\rm tree}
  =
  2
  \bigl(p^{\mu_1}\cdots p^{\mu_N}-\text{traces}\bigr),
\]
so
\[
  a_{g,N}^{\,g,{\rm tree}}=1,
  \qquad
  a_{q_j,N}^{\,g,{\rm tree}}=0 .
\]
Equivalently, the tree-level parton distributions are
\(f_{q_j/q_i}(x)=\delta_{ij}\delta(1-x)\),
\(f_{g/g}(x)=\delta(1-x)\), and the mixed quark-gluon distributions vanish.

Expanding the light-ray operator near zero separation gives these local
operators contracted with the lightlike vector \(n^\mu\).  Consequently the
integer moments of the parton distributions are the same reduced matrix
elements, with the precise flavor and charge-conjugation combination fixed by
the current channel:
\[
  a_{a,N}(\mu)
  =
  \int_0^1 \dd x\,x^{N-1} f_{a/H}(x,\mu)
\]
for the corresponding parton combination.  The local OPE and the nonlocal
light-ray factorization are therefore two presentations of the same
short-distance data.

Renormalization mixes operators with the same spin, twist, and quantum
numbers.  Taking integer moments of \eqref{eq:dglap-evolution} gives
\[
  \mu\frac{\dd}{\dd\mu}a_{a,N}(\mu)
  =
  \sum_b P_{ab,N}(g(\mu))\,a_{b,N}(\mu),
  \qquad
  P_{ab,N}(g)=\int_0^1\dd x\,x^{N-1}P_{ab}(x,g).
\]
The coefficient functions in the current OPE obey the dual evolution so that
the physical structure functions are independent of the auxiliary
factorization scale.  This is the local-operator form of perturbative
scaling violation in deep inelastic scattering.

For a spin-\(N\) leading-twist operator whose scaling dimension near the
ultraviolet is
\[
  \Delta_N(g)=N+2+\tau_{1,N}g^2+O(g^4),
\]
the one-loop running
\[
  \frac{\dd}{\dd\log\mu}\frac1{g^2(\mu)}
  =
  \frac{b}{2\pi^2}+O(g^2)
\]
implies logarithmic scaling of its matrix element and Wilson coefficient.
Equivalently, with
\[
  \omega:=\frac{2P\cdot q}{Q^2}=\frac1{x_B},
\]
the leading large-\(Q\) term in the \(N\)-th moment contribution has the form
\begin{equation}
  W_N(Q,\omega)
  \propto
  \omega^N
  \left(
    \log\frac{Q}{\Lambda}
  \right)^{-2\pi^2\tau_{1,N}/b}
  \left[1+O\!\left(\frac1{\log(Q/\Lambda)}\right)\right],
  \label{eq:qcd-dis-logarithmic-scaling-violation}
\end{equation}
up to the matrix of anomalous dimensions when several operators mix.  The
power \(\omega^N\) is the Bjorken-scaling kinematic dependence of a spin-\(N\)
operator; asymptotic freedom turns exact scaling into logarithmic scaling
violation.

\section{Cusped Wilson Lines, Large Spin, and the DIS Endpoint}
\label{sec:qcd-cusped-wilson-lines-large-spin}

The compact-\(x\) factorization statement in
Hypothesis~\ref{hyp:qcd-dis-leading-twist-factorization} deliberately does not
claim uniform control as \(x_B\to1\).  In that endpoint region the invariant
mass of the hadronic final state,
\[
  W_X^2=(P+q)^2
  =
  M^2+Q^2\left(\frac1{x_B}-1\right),
\]
is much smaller than \(Q^2\).  The short-distance coefficient functions then
contain distributions supported near \(x=1\), such as
\([\log^k(1-x)/(1-x)]_+\), and the perturbative expansion must be reorganized.
The universal coefficient controlling the leading endpoint singularity is
most cleanly defined by a cusped Wilson-line operator.  This definition is an
operator statement, not a parton-model picture.

Let \(R\) be a finite-dimensional unitary representation of \(SU(N_c)\), with
quadratic Casimir \(C_R\) in the trace-delta convention
\eqref{eq:qcd-casimir-definitions}.  In Euclidean signature choose two unit
vectors \(e_1,e_2\) with \(e_1\cdot e_2=\cos\phi\), \(0<\phi<\pi\), and let
\(\Gamma_{\phi,L,\epsilon}\) be the two-segment path
\[
  x(s)=-s e_1,\quad \epsilon\le s\le L,
  \qquad
  x(t)=t e_2,\quad \epsilon\le t\le L,
\]
joined at the origin after removing a ball of radius \(\epsilon\).  The
regulated cusped line is the path-ordered transporter
\[
  W_R(\Gamma_{\phi,L,\epsilon})
  =
  \mathcal P
  \exp\left\{\ii\int_{\Gamma_{\phi,L,\epsilon}}
  A_\mu^a(x)t_R^a\,\dd x^\mu\right\}.
\]
The straight-line endpoint and perimeter renormalizations are fixed by the
same regulator applied to a smooth line.  After these local smooth-line
renormalizations are divided out, the remaining logarithmic singularity at
the corner defines the Euclidean cusp coefficient \(K_R(\phi,g)\) by
\begin{equation}
  -\log\langle W_R(\Gamma_{\phi,L,\epsilon})\rangle_{\rm smooth-subtracted}
  =
  K_R(\phi,g)\log\frac{L}{\epsilon}+O(1).
  \label{eq:qcd-euclidean-cusp-coefficient-definition}
\end{equation}
The minus sign in this definition is part of the convention: it combines with
the \(i^2\) from expanding the Wilson transporter, so that the one-loop
coefficient below is read directly from the positive Euclidean propagator.
For timelike Wilson lines in Lorentzian signature we instead use the
positive lightlike cusp anomalous dimension
\[
  \Gamma_{\rm cusp}^R(g)
  :=
  \lim_{\chi\to\infty}
  \frac{\Gamma_{\rm cusp}^R(\chi,g)}{\chi},
  \qquad
  -v_1\cdot v_2=\cosh\chi,\quad v_1^2=v_2^2=-1,
\]
where \(\Gamma_{\rm cusp}^R(\chi,g)\) is obtained from the same local
renormalization problem by the analytic continuation \(\phi=\ii\chi\).
This convention is the one used in
\eqref{eq:qcd-quark-cusp-one-loop-trace-delta}.

At one loop the angular dependence follows from a single eikonal exchange.
In Feynman gauge, with the connection normalization of this chapter, the
Euclidean short-distance propagator is
\[
  \langle A_\mu^a(x)A_\nu^b(0)\rangle
  =
  \frac{g^2\delta^{ab}\delta_{\mu\nu}}{4\pi^2x^2}+O(g^4).
\]
The exchange between the two rays contributes, up to cutoff-independent
finite terms, the following scale-invariant part of
\(-\log\langle W_R\rangle\):
\[
  \frac{g^2C_R}{4\pi^2}
  \int_\epsilon^L\dd s\int_\epsilon^L\dd t\,
  \frac{e_1\cdot e_2}
  {s^2+t^2+2st\,e_1\cdot e_2}.
\]
The coefficient of \(\log(L/\epsilon)\) is local at the cusp, so set
\(t=sr\) in the scale-invariant part.  The angular integral is
\begin{equation}
  J(\phi)
  :=
  \int_0^\infty
  \frac{\cos\phi\,\dd r}
  {r^2+2r\cos\phi+1}
  =
  \phi\cot\phi .
  \label{eq:qcd-cusp-angular-integral}
\end{equation}
Indeed,
\[
  r^2+2r\cos\phi+1
  =
  (r+\cos\phi)^2+\sin^2\phi,
\]
and the antiderivative is
\[
  \cot\phi\,
  \arctan\frac{r+\cos\phi}{\sin\phi}.
\]
Taking \(r\) from \(0\) to \(\infty\) gives
\(\cot\phi(\pi/2-\arctan(\cot\phi))=\phi\cot\phi\), with the principal branch
of \(\arctan\) for \(0<\phi<\pi\).  A smooth straight line corresponds to
\(\phi=0\), for which \(J(0)=1\).  Therefore the corner-specific logarithm is
\begin{equation}
  K_R^{(1)}(\phi,g)
  =
  \frac{g^2C_R}{4\pi^2}
  \bigl(\phi\cot\phi-1\bigr).
  \label{eq:qcd-euclidean-cusp-one-loop}
\end{equation}
Continuing \(\phi=\ii\chi\) gives the Lorentzian timelike cusp coefficient
\begin{equation}
  \Gamma_{\rm cusp}^R(\chi,g)
  =
  \frac{g^2C_R}{4\pi^2}
  \bigl(\chi\coth\chi-1\bigr)+O(g^4),
  \qquad
  \Gamma_{\rm cusp}^R(g)
  =
  \frac{g^2C_R}{4\pi^2}+O(g^4).
  \label{eq:qcd-lorentzian-cusp-one-loop}
\end{equation}
For \(R=\square\), this is exactly the quark coefficient in
\eqref{eq:qcd-quark-cusp-one-loop-trace-delta}.

The same coefficient appears in the endpoint renormalization of light-ray
operators.  Near \(x=1\), the emitted radiation is soft compared with the
parent quark momentum, and the Wilson transporter in the light-ray operator
is approximated by eikonal propagation along the two nearly lightlike
directions selected by the incoming hadron and the hard current.  The local
renormalization of the resulting lightlike cusp supplies the coefficient of
the plus distribution \(D_0=(1-x)^{-1}_+\) in the nonsinglet kernel:
\begin{equation}
  P_{\rm ns}(x,g)
  =
  \Gamma_{\rm cusp}^q(g)\,D_0(x)
  +\text{terms with less singular Mellin growth as }x\to1 .
  \label{eq:qcd-endpoint-kernel-cusp-coefficient}
\end{equation}
Equation \eqref{eq:qcd-endpoint-kernel-cusp-coefficient} is meant as a
perturbative renormalization statement for the specified light-ray operator
and subtraction scheme.  It is not a nonperturbative theorem about the full
endpoint region of every DIS observable; threshold factorization also
requires a hard coefficient, a jet function, a soft matrix element, a
rapidity prescription when appropriate, and a controlled power remainder.

The Mellin transform turns this endpoint problem into a large-spin problem.
For a distribution \(K(x)\), define
\[
  K_N:=\int_0^1\dd x\,x^{N-1}K(x).
\]
Using the \(D_0\) convention above, the nonsinglet one-loop kernel has the
exact moment
\begin{equation}
  P_{{\rm ns},N}^{(0)}
  =
  C_F\left[
    -2H_{N-1}-\frac1N-\frac1{N+1}+\frac32
  \right],
  \qquad N\ge1 .
  \label{eq:qcd-nonsinglet-moment-exact}
\end{equation}
The harmonic number appears because
\begin{equation}
  \int_0^1\dd x\,x^{N-1}D_0(x)
  =
  \int_0^1\dd x\,
  \frac{x^{N-1}-1}{1-x}
  =
  -H_{N-1}.
  \label{eq:qcd-d0-mellin-harmonic}
\end{equation}
The last equality follows by writing
\((1-x^{N-1})/(1-x)=1+x+\cdots+x^{N-2}\) before integration.  Thus
\eqref{eq:qcd-nonsinglet-moment-exact} gives quark-number conservation at
\(N=1\), and at large \(N\)
\begin{equation}
  P_{{\rm ns},N}^{(0)}
  =
  -2C_F\log N
  +C_F\left(\frac32-2\gamma_{\rm E}\right)
  +O(N^{-1}).
  \label{eq:qcd-nonsinglet-large-n}
\end{equation}
Therefore, with \(P=(g^2/8\pi^2)P^{(0)}+O(g^4)\),
\begin{equation}
  P_{{\rm ns},N}(g)
  =
  -\Gamma_{\rm cusp}^q(g)\log N+O(N^0),
  \qquad
  \Gamma_{\rm cusp}^q(g)
  =
  \frac{g^2C_F}{4\pi^2}+O(g^4).
  \label{eq:qcd-large-spin-dglap-cusp}
\end{equation}
This is the evolution eigenvalue for matrix elements in the convention
\(\mu\,\dd a_N/\dd\mu=P_Na_N\).  If instead one writes the anomalous dimension
of the local twist-two operator itself by
\(\mu\,\dd\mathcal O_N/\dd\mu=-\gamma_N^{\mathcal O}\mathcal O_N+\cdots\),
then
\[
  \gamma_N^{\mathcal O}(g)
  =
  \Gamma_{\rm cusp}^q(g)\log N+O(N^0).
\]
The sign difference is bookkeeping: coefficient functions and operator
matrix elements obey dual renormalization-group equations so that the
physical hadronic tensor is independent of the factorization scale.

The product \(g^2C_R\) in
\eqref{eq:qcd-lorentzian-cusp-one-loop} is invariant under generator-coordinate
changes.  If \(T^a\) denotes the common half-trace generator and
\(g_{\rm ht}=\sqrt2\,g\), then
\[
  g^2C_F=g_{\rm ht}^2C_F^{\rm ht}.
\]
The cusp coefficient is thus independent of the choice of generator
coordinates once the coupling coordinate is transformed with the action.

Endpoint resummation is not a new definition of the PDF.  It is an additional
factorization of the coefficient function in a region where the compact-\(x\)
remainder estimate is not uniform.  A precise threshold factorization theorem
must specify the hard current coefficient, the jet function for the nearly
collinear final-state radiation, the soft Wilson-line matrix element, the
contour prescription for Mellin or Laplace inversion, and a remainder estimate
in the joint limit \(Q\to\infty\), \(N\to\infty\).  The large-spin logarithm in
\eqref{eq:qcd-large-spin-dglap-cusp} is the operator-renormalization side of
that structure.  The same Wilson-line cusp observable is the QCD analogue of
the large-spin cusp scaling function studied in
Chapter~\ref{ch:planar-n4-asymptotic-bethe-ansatz}, but the logical status is
different: in QCD it is a perturbative Wilson-line anomalous dimension tied
to factorization hypotheses, while in planar \(\mathcal N=4\) super
Yang--Mills the BES equation gives an integrability construction of the
planar large-spin function in a special conformal theory.  The small-\(x\)
limit is a different boundary of the same DIS observable and is not obtained
by continuing the threshold expansion.

\begin{controlledapproximation}[Tested DIS threshold plus-distribution window]
\label{ca:qcd-dis-threshold-plus-distribution-window}
Let
\[
  D_k(x):=\left[\frac{\log^k(1-x)}{1-x}\right]_+,\qquad k\ge0,
\]
so that, for a smooth test function \(\varphi\),
\begin{equation}
  \langle D_k,\varphi\rangle
  =
  \int_0^1
  \frac{\log^k(1-x)}{1-x}
  \bigl(\varphi(x)-\varphi(1)\bigr)\,\dd x .
  \label{eq:qcd-dis-threshold-plus-action}
\end{equation}
The subtraction is the physical endpoint datum.  The cusp kernel sees the
variation of the measured weight near \(x=1\), not the value of the inclusive
bin at the elastic endpoint.  On a terminal cell \(1-\delta\le x\le1\), with
\(0<\delta<1\),
\begin{align}
  \left|\int_{1-\delta}^1
  \frac{\varphi(x)-\varphi(1)}{1-x}\,\dd x\right|
  &\le
  \|\varphi'\|_{L^\infty([1-\delta,1])}\,\delta,
  \label{eq:qcd-dis-d0-terminal-bound}\\
  \left|\int_{1-\delta}^1
  \frac{\log(1-x)}{1-x}
  \bigl(\varphi(x)-\varphi(1)\bigr)\,\dd x\right|
  &\le
  \|\varphi'\|_{L^\infty([1-\delta,1])}\,
  \delta\bigl(1+|\log\delta|\bigr).
  \label{eq:qcd-dis-d1-terminal-bound}
\end{align}
Equivalently, writing \(u=1-x\) and
\(\varphi(1-u)=\varphi(1)-a_1u+a_2u^2+O(u^3)\), the terminal cell contributes
\begin{align}
  \langle D_0,\varphi\rangle_{\delta}
  &=
  -a_1\delta+\frac{a_2}{2}\delta^2+O(\delta^3),
  \label{eq:qcd-dis-d0-terminal-taylor}\\
  \langle D_1,\varphi\rangle_{\delta}
  &=
  -a_1\delta(\log\delta-1)
  +\frac{a_2}{2}\delta^2\left(\log\delta-\frac12\right)
  +O(\delta^3|\log\delta|).
  \label{eq:qcd-dis-d1-terminal-taylor}
\end{align}
Thus the Mellin identity
\[
  \langle D_0,x^{N-1}\rangle=-H_{N-1}
  =
  -\log N-\gamma_{\rm E}+O(N^{-1})
\]
is a large-\(N\) probe of a moving family of test functions.  It is not a
uniform consequence of the compact-\(x\) factorization statement on fixed test
functions supported away from \(x=1\).

A threshold theorem for DIS must therefore carry more data than the cusp
coefficient.  A moment-space coordinate for the approximation datum is
\begin{equation}
  F_{i,N}^{\rm thr}(Q)
  =
  H_i(Q,\mu_h)\,
  U_H\,
  J_{i,N}(Q,\mu_j)\,
  U_J\,
  S_{i,N}(\mu_s)\,
  U_S\,
  f_N(\mu_f)
  +R_{i,N}^{\rm thr},
  \label{eq:qcd-dis-threshold-factorized-coordinate}
\end{equation}
where the hard current, final-state jet, soft Wilson-line matrix element,
evolution kernels, Mellin-inversion contour or prescription, and remainder
\(R_{i,N}^{\rm thr}\) are part of the approximation datum.  The cusp anomalous
dimension controls the leading double-logarithmic evolution shared by these
factors; it does not by itself determine the finite matching constants, the
soft measurement convention, the prescription near the Landau boundary, or the
power remainders such as \(1/N\) and \(N\Lambda_{\rm QCD}/Q\).
\end{controlledapproximation}

\section{\texorpdfstring{Small-\(x\) Boundary, Dipoles, and the BFKL Kernel}
{Small-x Boundary, Dipoles, and the BFKL Kernel}}
\label{sec:qcd-small-x-bfkl}

The compact-\(x_B\) factorization hypothesis in
Hypothesis~\ref{hyp:qcd-dis-leading-twist-factorization} also does not claim
uniform control as \(x_B\to0\).  Put
\[
  Y:=\log\frac1{x_B},
\]
up to a process-dependent finite shift fixed by the chosen rapidity
factorization scheme.  In the high-energy boundary \(Y\gg1\), powers of
\(g^2Y\) may compete with powers of \(g^2\).  The leading-logarithmic
small-\(x\) expansion is therefore not a new definition of QCD and not a
replacement for the hadronic tensor
\eqref{eq:hadronic-tensor-definition}.  It is a rapidity factorization of
regulated Wilson-line matrix elements in a kinematic region where the
ordinary twist-two evolution at fixed \(x_B\) is not uniform.
The primary sources for the leading high-energy kernel and its Mellin
eigenvalue are the BFKL papers of Kuraev--Lipatov--Fadin and
Balitsky--Lipatov
\cite{KuraevFadinLipatov1977,BalitskyLipatov1978}.

Let \(\mathbf x,\mathbf y,\mathbf z\in\mathbb R^2\) denote transverse
coordinates, and set
\[
  r_{xy}:=|\mathbf x-\mathbf y|.
\]
The leading transverse kernel is
\begin{equation}
  M_{\mathbf x\mathbf y\mathbf z}
  :=
  \frac{r_{xy}^2}{r_{xz}^2 r_{zy}^2}.
  \label{eq:qcd-dipole-transverse-kernel}
\end{equation}
The singularities at \(\mathbf z=\mathbf x\) and
\(\mathbf z=\mathbf y\) are not meaningful as separate real-emission
probabilities.  They are defined only after the virtual subtraction in the
evolution equation, or after an equivalent rapidity regulator and finite-part
map have been specified.

\begin{definition}[Regulated small-\(x\) dipole setup]
\label{def:qcd-small-x-dipole-setup}
A small-\(x\) dipole setup for a color-singlet probe consists of:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item a QCD regulator \(\mathcal R_\epsilon\) preserving background gauge
  covariance, or a gauge-fixed BV regulator with the quantum master equation;
  \item two nearly lightlike Wilson-line directions, a rapidity regulator
  \(\rho\), and a finite rapidity separation \(Y\);
  \item fundamental Wilson transporters
  \(U_{\rho,Y}(\mathbf x)\) and \(U_{\rho,Y}(\mathbf y)\), with transverse
  closures or boundary conditions that make the residual endpoint gauge
  transformations explicit;
  \item the renormalized color dipole matrix element
  \begin{equation}
    S_Y(\mathbf x,\mathbf y)
    :=
    \frac1{N_c}
    \left\langle
      \operatorname{tr}_{\square}
      \bigl[
        U_{\rho,Y}(\mathbf x)U_{\rho,Y}(\mathbf y)^\dagger
      \bigr]
    \right\rangle_{\epsilon,\rho,Y}^{\rm ren},
    \qquad
    N_Y(\mathbf x,\mathbf y):=1-S_Y(\mathbf x,\mathbf y);
    \label{eq:qcd-small-x-dipole-definition}
  \end{equation}
  \item a finite-part prescription for ultraviolet endpoint divergences,
  lightlike or near-lightlike rapidity divergences, and possible cusp
  divergences introduced by the chosen closures.
\end{enumerate}
The quantity \(S_Y\) is a Wilson-line matrix element.  Its connection to a
measured small-\(x\) structure function is an additional high-energy
factorization statement with its own impact factor, rapidity subtraction, and
power-remainder data.
\end{definition}

At finite regulator, the trace in
\eqref{eq:qcd-small-x-dipole-definition} is gauge invariant after the
endpoint closures or boundary conditions of
Definition~\ref{def:qcd-small-x-dipole-setup} are included.  A background
gauge-covariant finite-part prescription therefore gives a gauge-invariant
renormalized dipole matrix element.
Each Wilson transporter transforms by multiplication with the gauge
transformation at its two endpoints.  Along a closed contour the adjacent
endpoint factors cancel and the residual base-point factor is removed by the
cyclicity of \(\operatorname{tr}_{\square}\).  If the regulated Wilson lines
are represented instead as open lines ending at a rapidity boundary, the
boundary condition is part of the setup and supplies the same endpoint
cancellation.  The finite-part map changes only regulator and scale
coordinates and is assumed background gauge covariant; hence it cannot
introduce an uncanceled color index.

\begin{hypothesis}[Leading-logarithmic dipole/BFKL evolution setup]
\label{hyp:qcd-leading-log-small-x-setup}
In a fixed-coupling leading-logarithmic rapidity scheme, the linearized
color-singlet dipole amplitude obeys
\begin{equation}
  \partial_Y N_Y(\mathbf x,\mathbf y)
  =
  \frac{g^2 C_A}{8\pi^3}
  \int_{\mathbb R^2}\dd^2\mathbf z\,
  M_{\mathbf x\mathbf y\mathbf z}
  \bigl[
    N_Y(\mathbf x,\mathbf z)
    +N_Y(\mathbf z,\mathbf y)
    -N_Y(\mathbf x,\mathbf y)
  \bigr],
  \label{eq:qcd-coordinate-bfkl-equation}
\end{equation}
where \(C_A=2N_c\) in the trace convention of
\eqref{eq:qcd-casimir-definitions}.  Equivalently, with
\[
  \bar\alpha_{\rm s}:=\frac{g^2C_A}{4\pi^2}
  =
  \frac{g^2N_c}{2\pi^2},
\]
the right-hand side is
\[
  \bar\alpha_{\rm s}\,
  \frac1{2\pi}
  \int\dd^2\mathbf z\,M_{\mathbf x\mathbf y\mathbf z}[\cdots].
\]
In the common half-trace convention,
\(g_{\rm ht}^2=2g^2\) and \(C_A^{\rm ht}=N_c\), so the product
\(g^2C_A=g_{\rm ht}^2C_A^{\rm ht}\) and the kernel coefficient are unchanged.

For a large-\(N_c\) dipole-factorization truncation of the Balitsky hierarchy,
the corresponding nonlinear equation for \(N_Y=1-S_Y\) has the additional
term
\begin{equation}
  -\frac{g^2 C_A}{8\pi^3}
  \int\dd^2\mathbf z\,
  M_{\mathbf x\mathbf y\mathbf z}\,
  N_Y(\mathbf x,\mathbf z)N_Y(\mathbf z,\mathbf y).
  \label{eq:qcd-bk-nonlinear-term}
\end{equation}
At finite \(N_c\), the exact Wilson-line rapidity hierarchy contains higher
multi-trace operators; replacing them by the product in
\eqref{eq:qcd-bk-nonlinear-term} is an additional closure assumption, not a
theorem about continuum QCD.
The dipole picture and its large-\(N_c\) small-\(x\) DIS evolution lineage are
traced through Mueller's onium construction, Balitsky's Wilson-line operator
expansion, and Kovchegov's \(F_2\) evolution equation
\cite{Mueller1994SoftGluons,Balitsky1996HighEnergy,
Kovchegov1999SmallXF2}.
\end{hypothesis}

\subsection{Finite Wilson-Line Evolution and the JIMWLK Theorem Boundary}
\label{subsec:qcd-finite-jimwlk-boundary}

The preceding dipole equation is a closed equation only after a truncation has
been chosen.  The more invariant small-\(x\) object is the rapidity evolution
of the whole algebra generated by Wilson lines.  To state the mathematical
content without importing the usual folklore terminology, first put the
Wilson-line variables on a finite transverse regulator.
The target-side Wilson renormalization group and its functional
Fokker--Planck form are the JIMWLK development
\cite{JalilianMarianKovnerLeonidovWeigert1998HighDensity,
JalilianMarianKovnerWeigert1998FiniteDensity,
IancuLeonidovMcLerran2001CGC,Weigert2002Unitarity}.

\begin{definition}[Finite Wilson-line rapidity-evolution generator]
\label{def:qcd-finite-wilson-line-rapidity-datum}
Let \(\Lambda_\perp\) be a finite set of transverse sites and let
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal C_{\Lambda_\perp}:=SU(N_c)^{\Lambda_\perp}
\end{equation}
with product Haar measure \(\dd h(U)\).  For
\(\mathbf x\in\Lambda_\perp\) and Lie-algebra index \(a\), let
\(L_{\mathbf x}^a\) be the left-invariant vector field
\begin{equation}
  (L_{\mathbf x}^aF)(U)
  :=
  \left.
  \frac{\dd}{\dd t}
  F(\ldots,\ee^{\ii t t^a}U_{\mathbf x},\ldots)
  \right|_{t=0},
  \label{eq:qcd-finite-wilson-left-derivative}
\end{equation}
where the generators obey the trace convention of
\eqref{eq:qcd-casimir-definitions}.  A finite Wilson-line
rapidity-evolution structure consists of a family of smooth coefficients
\[
  \mathcal A_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{ab}(U;Y)
  =
  \mathcal A_{\mathbf y\mathbf x}^{ba}(U;Y)
\]
whose finite matrix
\((\mathcal A_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{ab})\) is nonnegative at each
\((U,Y)\), is equivariant under residual global color conjugation, and is
defined in the same rapidity and ultraviolet regulator scheme as the Wilson
lines.  The corresponding finite Wilson-line generator is the
second-order operator
\begin{equation}
  (\mathcal H_YF)(U)
  :=
  \frac12
  \sum_{\mathbf x,\mathbf y\in\Lambda_\perp}
  \sum_{a,b}
  L_{\mathbf x}^a
  \left[
    \mathcal A_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{ab}(U;Y)
    L_{\mathbf y}^bF(U)
  \right].
  \label{eq:qcd-finite-wilson-line-generator}
\end{equation}
This finite generator is a finite-regulator replacement for the JIMWLK Hamiltonian only
after the coefficient \(\mathcal A\) has been derived from a specified QCD
rapidity factorization scheme.
\end{definition}

The finite object has two equivalent presentations.  In the weak, or
observable, presentation a rapidity-dependent state \(W_Y\dd h\) on
\(\mathcal C_{\Lambda_\perp}\) obeys
\begin{equation}
  \frac{\dd}{\dd Y}
  \int_{\mathcal C_{\Lambda_\perp}}F(U)\,W_Y(U)\,\dd h(U)
  =
  \int_{\mathcal C_{\Lambda_\perp}}(\mathcal H_YF)(U)\,W_Y(U)\,\dd h(U)
  \label{eq:qcd-finite-jimwlk-weak-form}
\end{equation}
for every smooth gauge-invariant Wilson-line observable \(F\).  In the strong,
or density, presentation
\begin{equation}
  \partial_YW_Y=\mathcal H_Y^*W_Y,
  \qquad
  \mathcal H_Y^*w
  =
  \frac12
  \sum_{\mathbf x,\mathbf y,a,b}
  L_{\mathbf y}^b
  \left[
    \mathcal A_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{ab}(U;Y)
    L_{\mathbf x}^aw
  \right],
  \label{eq:qcd-finite-jimwlk-density-form}
\end{equation}
where the adjoint is taken with respect to product Haar measure.  The two
forms are identical by integration by parts on the compact Lie group:
\[
  \int (L_{\mathbf x}^aF)G\,\dd h
  =
  -\int F(L_{\mathbf x}^aG)\,\dd h .
\]
Applying this identity twice to
\eqref{eq:qcd-finite-wilson-line-generator} gives
\eqref{eq:qcd-finite-jimwlk-density-form}.  Since
\(\mathcal H_Y1=0\), the total mass \(\int W_Y\dd h\) is conserved.  Since
the coefficient matrix is nonnegative, the principal symbol is that of a
diffusion on the compact manifold.  When the closure of \(\mathcal H_Y\) on
continuous functions satisfies the positive maximum principle, the
Hille--Yosida theorem gives the corresponding Markov semigroup.  This
semigroup assertion belongs to ordinary finite-dimensional diffusion analysis;
the QCD-specific work is the derivation and limiting control of
\(\mathcal A\).  These are finite statements about
\(\mathcal C_{\Lambda_\perp}\), not continuum QCD theorems.

For the dipole observable
\begin{equation}
  F_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}(U)
  :=
  \frac1{N_c}
  \operatorname{tr}_{\square}(U_{\mathbf x}U_{\mathbf y}^\dagger),
  \label{eq:qcd-finite-dipole-observable}
\end{equation}
the weak equation
\eqref{eq:qcd-finite-jimwlk-weak-form} generates the first equation of the
Balitsky hierarchy.  The right-hand side contains Wilson-line observables with
more transverse labels.  A closed BK equation is obtained only after
large-\(N_c\), mean-field, or other factorization input has replaced those
higher observables by products of dipoles.  Thus the logical chain is
\[
  \text{finite Wilson-line diffusion}
  \longrightarrow
  \text{Balitsky hierarchy}
  \longrightarrow
  \text{BK closure under extra hypotheses},
\]
not a direct theorem from the BFKL eigenvalue calculation.

\paragraph{Dipole projection and the BK closure boundary.}
The first arrow in the preceding display is an operator statement on the
finite Wilson-line algebra; the second arrow is a closure operation on a
subsystem of expectation values.  It is useful to write this subsystem before
passing to the continuum transverse plane.  Fix a finite transverse set
\(\Lambda_\perp\) and nonnegative cell weights
\(\omega_{\mathbf x\mathbf y;\mathbf z}(Y)\), symmetric under
\(\mathbf x\leftrightarrow\mathbf y\), which represent the regulated
cell-integrals of the leading dipole kernel in the same rapidity scheme as
\(\mathcal H_Y\).  For distinct transverse labels set
\[
  S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}(Y)
  :=
  \langle F_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}\rangle_Y,
  \qquad
  S_{\mathbf x\mathbf z;\mathbf z\mathbf y}^{(2)}(Y)
  :=
  \langle
    F_{\mathbf x\mathbf z}F_{\mathbf z\mathbf y}
  \rangle_Y ,
\]
where the expectation is the weak Wilson-line state in
\eqref{eq:qcd-finite-jimwlk-weak-form}.  A finite leading-logarithmic dipole
projection is the assertion that
\begin{equation}
  \partial_Y S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}
  =
  \sum_{\mathbf z\in\Lambda_\perp\setminus\{\mathbf x,\mathbf y\}}
  \omega_{\mathbf x\mathbf y;\mathbf z}
  \left(
    S_{\mathbf x\mathbf z;\mathbf z\mathbf y}^{(2)}
    -
    S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}
  \right).
  \label{eq:qcd-finite-balitsky-first-dipole-projection}
\end{equation}
Equation~\eqref{eq:qcd-finite-balitsky-first-dipole-projection} is not a
closed equation for \(S^{(1)}\).  Its new variable is the double-dipole
expectation \(S^{(2)}\), whose rapidity derivative involves still higher
Wilson-line observables.  This is the finite-regulator form of the Balitsky
hierarchy.

The BK reduction is a controlled approximation only if one supplies a
decorrelation estimate for the connected double-dipole coordinate
\[
  C_{\mathbf x\mathbf z;\mathbf z\mathbf y}(Y)
  :=
  S_{\mathbf x\mathbf z;\mathbf z\mathbf y}^{(2)}(Y)
  -
  S_{\mathbf x\mathbf z}^{(1)}(Y)S_{\mathbf z\mathbf y}^{(1)}(Y).
\]
With
\begin{equation}
  E_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{\rm BK}(Y)
  :=
  \sum_{\mathbf z\ne\mathbf x,\mathbf y}
  \omega_{\mathbf x\mathbf y;\mathbf z}(Y)\,
  C_{\mathbf x\mathbf z;\mathbf z\mathbf y}(Y),
  \label{eq:qcd-bk-closure-error-coordinate}
\end{equation}
the exact first dipole equation is
\[
  \partial_Y S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}
  =
  \sum_{\mathbf z\ne\mathbf x,\mathbf y}
  \omega_{\mathbf x\mathbf y;\mathbf z}
  \left(
    S_{\mathbf x\mathbf z}^{(1)}S_{\mathbf z\mathbf y}^{(1)}
    -
    S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}
  \right)
  +E_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{\rm BK}.
\]
Thus the phrase ``large-\(N_c\) BK equation'' means that a separate argument
has made \(E^{\rm BK}\) negligible in the norm and rapidity interval under
discussion.  In the amplitude variable
\(N_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}=1-S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}\), the closed
finite BK equation obtained by setting \(E^{\rm BK}=0\) is
\begin{equation}
  \partial_Y N_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}
  =
  \sum_{\mathbf z\ne\mathbf x,\mathbf y}
  \omega_{\mathbf x\mathbf y;\mathbf z}
  \left(
    N_{\mathbf x\mathbf z}
    +N_{\mathbf z\mathbf y}
    -N_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}
    -N_{\mathbf x\mathbf z}N_{\mathbf z\mathbf y}
  \right).
  \label{eq:qcd-finite-bk-amplitude-equation}
\end{equation}
This is the finite-cell version of
\eqref{eq:qcd-coordinate-bfkl-equation} plus
\eqref{eq:qcd-bk-nonlinear-term}.

The finite error estimate is elementary but important.  Suppose a real
mean-field solution \(\widetilde S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}\) and the exact first
coordinates \(S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}\) both take values in
\([0,1]\) on a rapidity interval \(0\le Y\le Y_*\), share the same initial
data, and have
\[
  L:=\sup_{Y\le Y_*}\sup_{\mathbf x\ne\mathbf y}
  \sum_{\mathbf z\ne\mathbf x,\mathbf y}
  \omega_{\mathbf x\mathbf y;\mathbf z}(Y)<\infty ,
  \qquad
  \varepsilon(Y):=
  \sup_{\mathbf x\ne\mathbf y}
  |E_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{\rm BK}(Y)|.
\]
Writing
\[
  d(Y):=
  \sup_{\mathbf x\ne\mathbf y}
  |S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}^{(1)}(Y)-\widetilde S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}(Y)|,
\]
one obtains
\begin{equation}
  d(Y)
  \le
  \int_0^Y \ee^{3L(Y-s)}\varepsilon(s)\,\dd s.
  \label{eq:qcd-finite-bk-closure-error-bound}
\end{equation}
Indeed, on the unit interval
\(|ab-a'b'|\le |a-a'|+|b-b'|\), so the product term contributes at most
\(2Ld(Y)\), the virtual term at most \(Ld(Y)\), and the connected covariance
term contributes \(\varepsilon(Y)\); Gronwall gives
\eqref{eq:qcd-finite-bk-closure-error-bound}.  The closed BK vector field
also points into the unit cube: at \(S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}=0\) its right-hand
side is nonnegative, while at \(S_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}=1\) it is
nonpositive.  This finite ODE fact explains the black-disk and transparent
fixed points of the closed approximation.  It does not replace the missing
QCD estimate on \(E^{\rm BK}\), nor the continuum limit of the cell weights.

The continuum JIMWLK problem is the removal of the finite transverse regulator
and rapidity regulator from this finite compact-manifold construction while
keeping the Wilson-line observables, the coefficient kernel, and the
factorization map to the measured process in a topology strong enough to pass
to the limit in \eqref{eq:qcd-finite-jimwlk-weak-form}.  The monograph
therefore treats the color-glass weight functional as a renormalized
Wilson-line state, not as a literal probability measure on classical gluon
fields unless such a measure has been constructed in the chosen regulator.

\paragraph{Projective continuum limit for Wilson-line states.}
A continuum statement of JIMWLK must be stronger than a sequence of finite
Balitsky equations.  Let
\(\{\Lambda_\perp^{(m)}\}_{m\in I}\) be a directed family of finite transverse
regulators and let
\(\mathcal A_m=C^\infty(SU(N_c)^{\Lambda_\perp^{(m)}})^{G_{\rm diag}}\) be
the gauge-invariant Wilson-line algebra on the \(m\)-th regulator.  A
cylinder observable \(F\) depends on a fixed finite list of transverse labels;
for all sufficiently fine regulators it has a representative \(F_m\in
\mathcal A_m\).  A projective Wilson-line state limit on
\([0,Y_*]\) consists of
\[
  \bigl(
    \omega_{m,Y},\mathcal H_{m,Y},F\mapsto F_m,
    \omega_Y,\mathcal H^{\rm cyl}_Y,\mathcal I_Y
  \bigr)
\]
with the following data.  First, the finite states are compatible on cylinder
observables:
\begin{equation}
  \sup_{0\le Y\le Y_*}
  |\omega_{m,Y}(F_m)-\omega_Y(F)|
  \le \delta_{m,F},
  \qquad
  \delta_{m,F}\longrightarrow0 .
  \label{eq:qcd-jimwlk-projective-state-convergence}
\end{equation}
Second, the finite generators converge on the same cylinder test:
\begin{equation}
  \sup_{0\le Y\le Y_*}
  \|
    \mathcal H_{m,Y}F_m-(\mathcal H^{\rm cyl}_YF)_m
  \|_\infty
  \le \varepsilon_{m,F},
  \qquad
  \varepsilon_{m,F}\longrightarrow0 .
  \label{eq:qcd-jimwlk-projective-generator-convergence}
\end{equation}
Third, the rapidity dependence is uniformly controlled on cylinder tests, so
the finite weak equations are equicontinuous in \(Y\) and limits may be
passed through rapidity integrals.  Fourth, positivity, normalization, and the
positive-maximum-principle/Markov property hold at finite \(m\) and are closed
under the cylinder limit.  Fifth, \(\mathcal I_Y\) is the process map: it
turns the Wilson-line state into the measured small-\(x\) observable with the
chosen impact factors, subtractions, and power-remainder topology.

Under these hypotheses the finite weak equation has a genuine cylinder
limit.  Indeed, for \(Y_0<Y_1\),
\[
  \omega_{m,Y_1}(F_m)-\omega_{m,Y_0}(F_m)
  =
  \int_{Y_0}^{Y_1}
  \omega_{m,s}(\mathcal H_{m,s}F_m)\,\dd s .
\]
After adding and subtracting the limiting terms one obtains the error ledger
\begin{equation}
\begin{aligned}
&
\left|
  \omega_{Y_1}(F)-\omega_{Y_0}(F)
  -
  \int_{Y_0}^{Y_1}
    \omega_s(\mathcal H^{\rm cyl}_sF)\,\dd s
\right|
\\
&\qquad\le
  2\delta_{m,F}
  +
  \int_{Y_0}^{Y_1}
  \bigl(
    \delta_{m,\mathcal H^{\rm cyl}F}(s)
    +
    \varepsilon_{m,F}(s)
  \bigr)\,\dd s ,
\end{aligned}
\label{eq:qcd-jimwlk-projective-weak-limit-budget}
\end{equation}
where the right-hand side is written with the corresponding uniform or
integrable bound on the state error for the generator observable when the
errors depend on \(s\).  Letting \(m\to\infty\)
gives the continuum weak equation on the cylinder algebra,
\[
  \omega_{Y_1}(F)-\omega_{Y_0}(F)
  =
  \int_{Y_0}^{Y_1}
    \omega_s(\mathcal H^{\rm cyl}_sF)\,\dd s .
\]
If \(Y\mapsto\omega_Y(F)\) is absolutely continuous, this is the
differential weak JIMWLK equation for cylinder observables.  The BK closure
estimate above controls one projected dipole subsystem and one connected
covariance coordinate; it does not supply
\eqref{eq:qcd-jimwlk-projective-state-convergence},
\eqref{eq:qcd-jimwlk-projective-generator-convergence}, or the process map.
Thus continuum JIMWLK is a projective theorem about Wilson-line states and
QCD factorization; the formal functional Hamiltonian names only the candidate
cylinder generator.

\begin{controlledapproximation}[Measured small-\(x\) observable map]
\label{ca:qcd-small-x-measured-observable-map}
The quantity compared with a DIS structure function, a diffractive cross
section, or a forward-production bin is a tested color-singlet QCD functional
rather than the Wilson-line state by itself.  Fix a compact small-\(x\)
window, a hard scale range \(Q\ge Q_0\), and a test function
\(\varphi\in\mathcal D_{\rm sx}\) for the measured kinematic bin.  Let
\(\mathfrak T_{Q,Y}[\varphi]\) denote the exact tested functional built from
the relevant current product or inclusive/diffractive cross-section operator
in a finite regulator.  The Wilson-line coordinate associated to the same bin
has the form
\begin{equation}
  \mathcal S^{\rm sx}_{Q,Y}[\varphi]
  :=
  \omega_Y\!\left(\Phi^\varphi_{Q,Y}\right),
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-wilson-line-observable-coordinate}
\end{equation}
where \(\Phi^\varphi_{Q,Y}\) is the gauge-invariant cylinder-limit Wilson-line
observable produced by the chosen high-energy operator product expansion,
impact factor, rapidity subtraction, and renormalization scheme.  For a
virtual photon this impact factor contains the hard \(q\bar q\) and higher
Fock-state fluctuation weights; for a hadronic final state it contains the
corresponding jet, fragmentation, or diffractive projection.  The transverse
Wilson-line evolution supplies only the target-side rapidity dependence in
this coordinate.

A controlled small-\(x\) prediction for the tested observable therefore
consists of:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item the exact finite-regulator definition of
  \(\mathfrak T_{Q,Y}[\varphi]\) in the measured channel;
  \item an impact-factor matching map from the exact color-singlet operator to
  \(\Phi^\varphi_{Q,Y}\), including virtual corrections, real-emission
  subtractions, and scheme conventions;
  \item a rapidity-factorization statement in which the separation scale
  dependence cancels between the impact factor and JIMWLK evolution in the
  declared topology;
  \item the projective Wilson-line state limit of
  \eqref{eq:qcd-jimwlk-projective-weak-limit-budget} on the cylinder
  representatives entering \(\Phi^\varphi_{Q,Y}\);
  \item an evolution and truncation estimate for the retained finite
  JIMWLK/Balitsky subsystem, with a separate closure residual when BK or a
  moment truncation is used;
  \item a power-remainder estimate for finite \(Q\), finite target size,
  finite rapidity interval, endpoint contamination, and omitted final-state
  measurements.
\end{enumerate}
The finite BFKL eigenvalue, the compact Wilson-line diffusion calculation, and
the BK closure estimate are entries in this list.  They become physics
predictions for the measured functional through the impact-factor map and the
residual controls above.
\end{controlledapproximation}

\begin{controlledapproximation}[Leading small-\(x\) inclusive DIS dipole channel]
\label{ca:qcd-small-x-leading-dis-dipole-channel}
For the inclusive virtual-photon channel the preceding abstract coordinate can
be made explicit at the leading \(q\bar q\) impact-factor level.  Write
\[
  \mathbf x=\mathbf b+\frac{\mathbf r}{2},
  \qquad
  \mathbf y=\mathbf b-\frac{\mathbf r}{2},
  \qquad
  \epsilon_f^2(z,Q):=z(1-z)Q^2+m_f^2 ,
\]
and let \(K_\nu\) denote the modified Bessel function.  In the usual dipole
normalization, following the virtual-photon dipole representation of
\cite{NikolaevZakharov1991,Kovchegov1999SmallXF2}, the squared photon
wave-function weights are
\begin{align}
  W_T^{(f)}(z,r;Q)
  &:=
  \frac{N_c\alpha_{\rm em}e_f^2}{2\pi^2}
  \left(
    [z^2+(1-z)^2]\epsilon_f^2 K_1^2(\epsilon_f r)
    +m_f^2K_0^2(\epsilon_f r)
  \right),
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-photon-wavefunction-transverse}
\\
  W_L^{(f)}(z,r;Q)
  &:=
  \frac{N_c\alpha_{\rm em}e_f^2}{2\pi^2}
  \left(
    4Q^2z^2(1-z)^2K_0^2(\epsilon_f r)
  \right).
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-photon-wavefunction-longitudinal}
\end{align}
The leading dipole coordinate for a finite tested bin
\(\varphi\in C_c^\infty((0,1)\times\mathbb R^2_{\mathbf r}
\times\mathbb R^2_{\mathbf b})\) is
\begin{align}
  \mathcal F_i^{\rm dip}[Q,Y_t;\varphi]
  &:=
  2\sum_f
  \int_0^1\dd z
  \int_{\mathbb R^2}\dd^2\mathbf r
  \int_{\mathbb R^2}\dd^2\mathbf b\,
  \varphi(z,\mathbf r,\mathbf b)\,
  W_i^{(f)}(z,r;Q)\,
  \notag
\\
  &\quad{}\times
  N_{Y_t}
  \left(
    \mathbf b+\frac{\mathbf r}{2},
    \mathbf b-\frac{\mathbf r}{2}
  \right),
  \qquad i=T,L .
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-leading-dis-dipole-functional}
\end{align}
Multiplication by the conventional factor \(Q^2/(4\pi^2\alpha_{\rm em})\)
turns the corresponding inclusive virtual-photon cross section into
\(F_T\) or \(F_L\).  Equation
\eqref{eq:qcd-small-x-leading-dis-dipole-functional} is therefore the first
nontrivial instance of \(\Phi^\varphi_{Q,Y}\): the Wilson-line state is
tested only after convolution with the physical photon fluctuation kernel and
the measured-bin function.

The rapidity split is part of the datum.  Let \(Y_s\) be the target rapidity
assigned to the Wilson-line state and let \(Y-Y_s\) be the projectile
rapidity assigned to the impact factor.  The factorized coordinate has the
form
\[
  \mathcal F_i(Y_s)
  =
  \omega_{Y_s}
  \left(
    \Phi_{i,Q,Y-Y_s}^{\varphi}
  \right).
\]
Using the weak JIMWLK equation gives
\begin{equation}
  \frac{\dd}{\dd Y_s}\mathcal F_i(Y_s)
  =
  \omega_{Y_s}
  \left(
    \mathcal H_{Y_s}\Phi_{i,Q,Y-Y_s}^{\varphi}
    -
    \partial_{Y_{\rm p}}
    \Phi_{i,Q,Y_{\rm p}}^{\varphi}\big|_{Y_{\rm p}=Y-Y_s}
  \right).
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-dis-rapidity-split-derivative}
\end{equation}
Thus independence of the artificial separation scale is not a slogan: at the
retained logarithmic order the projectile impact-factor subtraction must obey
the same cylinder generator as the target evolution, with the opposite
allocation of rapidity.  If the two sides differ by a cylinder observable
\(R^{\rm rap}_{i,Q,Y_s,\varphi}\), the rapidity residual in the measured DIS
bin is the integral of
\(\omega_{Y_s}(R^{\rm rap}_{i,Q,Y_s,\varphi})\) over the allowed separation
interval.

The BK or finite-moment error is propagated through the same physical kernel.
If a dipole approximation \(\widetilde N_{Y_t}\) satisfies
\[
  |N_{Y_t}(\mathbf b+\mathbf r/2,\mathbf b-\mathbf r/2)
    -\widetilde N_{Y_t}(\mathbf b+\mathbf r/2,\mathbf b-\mathbf r/2)|
  \leq e_N(Y_t;\mathbf r,\mathbf b),
\]
then
\begin{equation}
  \left|
    \mathcal F_i^{\rm dip}[Q,Y_t;\varphi]
    -
    \widetilde{\mathcal F}_i^{\rm dip}[Q,Y_t;\varphi]
  \right|
  \leq
  2\sum_f
  \int\dd z\,\dd^2\mathbf r\,\dd^2\mathbf b\,
  |\varphi|\,W_i^{(f)}(z,r;Q)\,
  e_N(Y_t;\mathbf r,\mathbf b).
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-dis-bk-error-propagation}
\end{equation}
In particular, a uniform finite-cell BK estimate such as
\eqref{eq:qcd-finite-bk-closure-error-bound} enters the measured structure
function multiplied by the photon-kernel norm of the tested bin, not as an
unweighted abstract residual.

Finally the endpoint and finite-\(Q\) pieces are separate estimates.  For a
regulated retained bin
\[
  \delta\le z\le1-\delta,\qquad
  \rho\le r\le R,\qquad
  \mathbf b\in B ,
\]
the discarded endpoint strips have the explicit spin-factor primitives
\begin{align}
  \int_0^\delta [z^2+(1-z)^2]\,\dd z
  &=
  \delta-\delta^2+\frac23\delta^3
  \le \delta,
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-transverse-endpoint-spin-bound}
\\
  \int_0^\delta 4z^2(1-z)^2\,\dd z
  &=
  \frac43\delta^3-2\delta^4+\frac45\delta^5
  \le \frac43\delta^3 ,
  \qquad 0\le\delta\le\frac12 .
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-longitudinal-endpoint-spin-bound}
\end{align}
On the retained bin the Bessel products appearing in \(W_i^{(f)}\) are bounded
by constants depending on \(Q,\delta,\rho,R\).  On the discarded endpoint
strips one must either keep the corresponding tail as an explicit residual or
use the relevant mass, collinear regulator, or integrable massless estimates
for the combinations \(\epsilon_f^2K_1^2(\epsilon_f r)\) and
\(z^2(1-z)^2K_0^2(\epsilon_f r)\).  With those constants, unitarity
\(0\le N_Y\le1\), and the impact-parameter support of \(\varphi\), the
displayed primitives give a finite endpoint remainder for the retained
observable.  The small-\(r\) complement is controlled instead by the
color-transparency input
\(N_Y(\mathbf b+\mathbf r/2,\mathbf b-\mathbf r/2)\le C_B(Y)r^2\) on the
target support; without such an input the dipole formula is only a formal
convolution near \(r=0\).  This is the sense in which the leading DIS dipole
formula is a measured-observable approximation with declared impact-factor,
rapidity, BK/JIMWLK, and finite-\(Q\) remainders, not just a statement about
Wilson-line geometry in transverse coordinate space.
\end{controlledapproximation}

\begin{proposition}[Measured small-\(x\) residual budget]
\label{prop:qcd-small-x-measured-residual-budget}
In the setting of
Controlled Approximation~\ref{ca:qcd-small-x-measured-observable-map}, suppose
the chosen finite approximation
\(\mathcal A^{(m,n)}_{Q,Y}[\varphi]\) to
\(\mathcal S^{\rm sx}_{Q,Y}[\varphi]\) satisfies
\begin{equation}
\begin{aligned}
  \mathfrak T_{Q,Y}[\varphi]
  =
  \mathcal A^{(m,n)}_{Q,Y}[\varphi]
  &+\Delta_{\rm IF}[\varphi]
  +\Delta_{\rm rap}[\varphi]
  +\Delta_{\rm proj}[\varphi]
\\
  &+\Delta_{\rm evol}[\varphi]
  +\Delta_{\rm clos}[\varphi]
  +\Delta_{\rm pwr}[\varphi],
\end{aligned}
\label{eq:qcd-small-x-measured-residual-decomposition}
\end{equation}
where the terms denote, respectively, impact-factor matching, rapidity
subtraction, projective regulator removal, finite evolution/truncation,
closure, and power remainders.  If, on the chosen window,
\begin{equation}
  |\Delta_a[\varphi]|
  \leq
  \epsilon_a(Q_0,Y_*)\,\|\varphi\|_{\mathcal D_{\rm sx}},
  \qquad
  a\in
  \{\mathrm{IF},\mathrm{rap},\mathrm{proj},
    \mathrm{evol},\mathrm{clos},\mathrm{pwr}\},
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-measured-residual-component-bounds}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
  \left|
    \mathfrak T_{Q,Y}[\varphi]
    -
    \mathcal A^{(m,n)}_{Q,Y}[\varphi]
  \right|
  \leq
  \left(
    \epsilon_{\rm IF}
    +\epsilon_{\rm rap}
    +\epsilon_{\rm proj}
    +\epsilon_{\rm evol}
    +\epsilon_{\rm clos}
    +\epsilon_{\rm pwr}
  \right)
  \|\varphi\|_{\mathcal D_{\rm sx}} .
  \label{eq:qcd-small-x-measured-residual-budget-bound}
\end{equation}
For full JIMWLK evolution one may set
\(\epsilon_{\rm clos}=0\) and keep the evolution error in
\(\epsilon_{\rm evol}\); for a BK or finite-moment projection,
\(\epsilon_{\rm clos}\) is the measured-channel image of the connected
Wilson-line residual such as \(E^{\rm BK}_{\mathbf x\mathbf y}\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Subtract the finite approximant in
\eqref{eq:qcd-small-x-measured-residual-decomposition} and apply the triangle
inequality with
\eqref{eq:qcd-small-x-measured-residual-component-bounds}.  The projective
term is controlled by the cylinder weak-equation ledger
\eqref{eq:qcd-jimwlk-projective-weak-limit-budget} after inserting the
cylinder representative of \(\Phi^\varphi_{Q,Y}\).  The impact-factor and
power terms are separate QCD statements about the measured color-singlet
operator, while the finite BFKL/Balitsky/JIMWLK calculations constrain their
corresponding coordinates inside the displayed sum.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Conformal covariance of the transverse kernel.}
The measure
\[
  \dd^2\mathbf z\,M_{\mathbf x\mathbf y\mathbf z}
\]
is invariant under translations, rotations, uniform scalings, and inversion
\(\mathbf u\mapsto\mathbf u/|\mathbf u|^2\), whenever the transformed points
avoid the inversion center.  Thus the leading fixed-coupling transverse
kernel is covariant under the real Mobius transformations generated by these
maps.

Translations and rotations preserve all distances and \(\dd^2\mathbf z\).
Under the scaling \(\mathbf u\mapsto\lambda\mathbf u\), the kernel
\(M_{\mathbf x\mathbf y\mathbf z}\) scales as \(\lambda^{-2}\), while
\(\dd^2\mathbf z\) scales as \(\lambda^2\).

For inversion, write \(\mathbf u'=\mathbf u/|\mathbf u|^2\).  The Euclidean
distance and area element transform as
\[
  |\mathbf u'-\mathbf v'|^2
  =
  \frac{|\mathbf u-\mathbf v|^2}
       {|\mathbf u|^2|\mathbf v|^2},
  \qquad
  \dd^2\mathbf z'
  =
  \frac{\dd^2\mathbf z}{|\mathbf z|^4}.
\]
Substituting these identities gives
\[
  M_{\mathbf x'\mathbf y'\mathbf z'}\,\dd^2\mathbf z'
  =
  \frac{
    r_{xy}^2/(|\mathbf x|^2|\mathbf y|^2)
  }{
    r_{xz}^2/(|\mathbf x|^2|\mathbf z|^2)\,
    r_{zy}^2/(|\mathbf z|^2|\mathbf y|^2)
  }
  \frac{\dd^2\mathbf z}{|\mathbf z|^4}
  =
  M_{\mathbf x\mathbf y\mathbf z}\,\dd^2\mathbf z.
\]
This proves the stated covariance.

\begin{proposition}[Mellin eigenvalue of the leading BFKL kernel]
\label{prop:qcd-bfkl-mellin-eigenvalue}
Let \(0<\operatorname{Re}\gamma<1\).  For the power
\[
  f_\gamma(\mathbf x,\mathbf y)
  =
  \bigl(\mu_\perp^2 r_{xy}^2\bigr)^\gamma ,
\]
define the subtracted transverse integral by analytic regularization as in
the proof below.  Then
\begin{equation}
  \frac1{2\pi}\int_{\mathbb R^2}\dd^2\mathbf z\,
  M_{\mathbf x\mathbf y\mathbf z}
  \bigl[
    f_\gamma(\mathbf x,\mathbf z)
    +f_\gamma(\mathbf z,\mathbf y)
    -f_\gamma(\mathbf x,\mathbf y)
  \bigr]
  =
  \chi(\gamma)f_\gamma(\mathbf x,\mathbf y),
  \label{eq:qcd-bfkl-eigenvalue-equation}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
  \chi(\gamma)
  =
  2\psi(1)-\psi(\gamma)-\psi(1-\gamma),
  \qquad
  \psi(u):=\frac{\dd}{\dd u}\log\Gamma(u).
  \label{eq:qcd-bfkl-characteristic-function}
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By translation and scaling it suffices to set
\(\mathbf y=0\), \(r_{xy}=1\), and \(\mu_\perp=1\).  The singular terms in
the three brackets are regulated in \(d=2-2\epsilon\) transverse dimensions.
The Euclidean beta integral
\begin{equation}
  \int\dd^d\mathbf z\,
  \frac1{(\mathbf z^2)^a((\mathbf z-\mathbf x)^2)^b}
  =
  \pi^{d/2}
  \frac{
    \Gamma(d/2-a)\Gamma(d/2-b)\Gamma(a+b-d/2)
  }{
    \Gamma(a)\Gamma(b)\Gamma(d-a-b)
  }
  (\mathbf x^2)^{d/2-a-b}
  \label{eq:qcd-bfkl-beta-integral}
\end{equation}
is first valid in its convergence domain and then analytically continued.
Applying \eqref{eq:qcd-bfkl-beta-integral} to the three pieces gives
\[
\begin{aligned}
  J_\epsilon(\gamma)
  &=
  \pi^{1-\epsilon}
  \frac{
    \Gamma(\gamma-\epsilon)\Gamma(-\epsilon)
    \Gamma(1-\gamma+\epsilon)
  }{
    \Gamma(1-\gamma)\Gamma(\gamma-2\epsilon)
  },\\
  J_\epsilon(1-\gamma)
  &=
  \pi^{1-\epsilon}
  \frac{
    \Gamma(1-\gamma-\epsilon)\Gamma(-\epsilon)
    \Gamma(\gamma+\epsilon)
  }{
    \Gamma(\gamma)\Gamma(1-\gamma-2\epsilon)
  },\\
  J_\epsilon(0)
  &=
  \pi^{1-\epsilon}
  \frac{\Gamma(-\epsilon)^2\Gamma(1+\epsilon)}
       {\Gamma(-2\epsilon)} .
\end{aligned}
\]
The regulated subtracted integral is
\[
  J_\epsilon(\gamma)+J_\epsilon(1-\gamma)-J_\epsilon(0).
\]
Using
\[
  \Gamma(u+\delta)
  =
  \Gamma(u)\bigl(1+\delta\psi(u)+O(\delta^2)\bigr)
\]
and the corresponding expansion at the pole \(u=0\), the \(1/\epsilon\)
terms cancel and the finite part is
\[
  2\pi\,[2\psi(1)-\psi(\gamma)-\psi(1-\gamma)].
\]
Dividing by \(2\pi\) proves
\eqref{eq:qcd-bfkl-eigenvalue-equation}.
\end{proof}

The leading fixed-coupling kernel in
\eqref{eq:qcd-coordinate-bfkl-equation} has
\[
  \chi\!\left(\frac12\right)=4\log2,
  \qquad
  \chi\!\left(\frac12+\ii\nu\right)
  =
  4\log2-14\zeta(3)\nu^2+O(\nu^4).
\]
These values follow from
\[
  \psi\!\left(\frac12\right)=-\gamma_{\rm E}-2\log2,
  \qquad
  \psi''\!\left(\frac12\right)=-14\zeta(3),
\]
inserted into \eqref{eq:qcd-bfkl-characteristic-function}; in particular
\(\chi'(\frac12)=0\) and \(\chi''(\frac12)=28\zeta(3)\).  A Mellin component
near \(\gamma=\frac12\) therefore evolves as
\[
  \exp\!\left[
    \bar\alpha_{\rm s}\,4\log2\,Y
    -\bar\alpha_{\rm s}\,14\zeta(3)\nu^2Y
    +O(\bar\alpha_{\rm s}\nu^4Y)
  \right]
\]
inside the fixed-coupling leading-logarithmic setup.

\begin{controlledapproximation}[Status of small-\(x\) resummation]
\label{ca:qcd-small-x-status}
A small-\(x\) resummation used for a QCD observable must state:
the regulated Wilson-line operator being evolved; the rapidity variable and
subtraction scheme; the impact factor connecting the Wilson-line matrix
element to the measured process; whether running coupling, energy-scale,
collinear-improvement, saturation, or finite-\(N_c\) hierarchy effects are
included; and the topology of the remainder estimate.  The BFKL kernel in
Proposition~\ref{prop:qcd-bfkl-mellin-eigenvalue} is a theorem about the
leading transverse integral.  Its physical use in high-energy hadron
scattering additionally requires the Wilson-line operator definition, impact
factor, rapidity-factorization scheme, and unitarization or saturation
framework appropriate to the observable.
\end{controlledapproximation}
